Obligation to Know the Three Categories
The poet declares the obligation on every mukallaf to know the three rational judgments for Allah and messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every mukallaf is obligated to know the three rational judgments (wājib, jā'iz, mumtani') concerning Allah and His messengers.

Core Idea
Knowing the three rational judgments about Allah and His messengers is an individual obligation.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid lays the foundational duty of every Muslim who has reached legal responsibility. Before delving into the details of divine attributes, the poet makes clear that knowledge of the necessary, possible, and impossible regarding Allah and His prophets is not optional but an obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the two-line Arabic poem once, then its English translation. Notice the parallel structure: first about Allah, then about His messengers.
2
Understand 'mukallaf'
The term 'mukallaf' refers to a person who has reached puberty and is sane, thus legally responsible under Islamic law. The obligation applies to every such individual.
This is a key concept in Islamic jurisprudence.
3
Identify the three categories
From the phrase 'مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا لِلَّهِ وَالْجَائِزَ وَالْمُمْتَنِعَا', identify the three rational judgments: necessary (wājib), possible (jā'iz), and impossible (mumtani').
These categories are the foundation of rational theology in Islam.
4
Extend to the messengers
The line 'وَمِثْلَ ذَا لِرُسْلِهِ' instructs that the same three categories apply to the prophets. Understand that this knowledge is required for both Allah and His messengers.
This covers the essential aspects of prophethood as well.
5
Reflect on the obligation
Consider why this knowledge is obligatory. It forms the basis for correct belief in Allah and His messengers, protecting one from false notions.
This obligation is not limited to scholars; it is for every mukallaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعًا وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا لِلَّهِ وَالْجَائِزَ وَالْمُمْتَنِعَا وَمِثْلَ ذَا لِرُسْلِهِ فَاسْتَمِعَا
For everyone who is legally charged (mukallaf), it is obligatory upon him to know what is obligatory for Allah, and the possible, and the impossible, and similarly for His messengers, so listen.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage states the core obligation of every mukallaf: to know the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mumtani') concerning Allah and the prophets. This is a foundational principle in Ash'ari kalam. The poet emphasizes that this knowledge is required by Islamic law.

Key Terms
تكليف
واجب
جائز
ممتنع
رسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think knowledge of Allah is only for scholars or the elite.
Correction: The poet asserts that knowing the three rational judgments is obligatory upon every mukallaf, regardless of scholarly status.

Checkpoint
What are the three categories a mukallaf must know about Allah?
A) Wajib, mustahabb, haram
B) Wajib, ja'iz, mumtani'
C) Wajib, sunnah, makruh
D) Wajib, ja'iz, mubah

Key Takeaway
The first duty of a mukallaf is to know what is necessary, possible, and impossible concerning Allah and His messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that knowing the three rational categories for Allah and His messengers is an individual obligation upon you. Have you ever consciously sought this knowledge, or have you been content with blind imitation? What will you change starting today to fulfill this duty?
?
The poet commands you to 'listen' – but have you truly listened with the intention to act? What distractions in your life prevent you from acquiring this essential knowledge?
?
If you were asked right now to name one thing that is wajib for Allah, one that is ja'iz, and one that is mumtani', could you answer? If not, how will you respond to your Lord when He asks why you neglected this obligation?

The Obligation of Knowing the Three Categories
The poet states the obligation to know the three categories for Allah and His messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every legally responsible Muslim is obligated to know the three rational categories (wajib, ja'iz, mumtani') as they apply to Allah and His messengers.

Core Idea
Knowing what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah is a personal obligation for every believer.

This lesson introduces the foundational obligation of Islamic theology: every mukallaf must learn the three essential categories of rational judgment—what is necessary (wajib), possible (ja'iz), and impossible (mumtani')—for Allah and His prophets. The poet emphasizes this duty with urgency, so we need to grasp its meaning and importance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic verse
Read the Arabic line carefully. Notice the key terms: كلف شرعا, وجب, واجب, جائز, ممتنع, رسل.
The verse is a single couplet; take time to absorb its rhythm.
2
Identify the obligation and its scope
The verse begins 'فكل من كلف شرعا وجبا عليه' – for every mukallaf it is obligatory. Ask: who is a mukallaf? What are they obligated to know? It specifies three categories for Allah and similarly for His messengers.
3
Connect to the three rational judgments
The three categories (wajib, ja'iz, mumtani') are the foundation of kalam. Relate this to the previous lesson where they were defined. Now we see their application is an obligation.
See lesson on rational judgments for definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكل من كلف شرعا وجبا عليه أن يعرف ما قد وجبا لله والجائز والممتنعا ومثل ذا لرسله فاستمعا
For everyone who is legally obligated (mukallaf) by the Law, it is necessarily incumbent upon him to know what is necessary (wajib) for Allah, and the possible (ja'iz) and the impossible (mumtani'), and similarly for His messengers, so listen.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the basic obligation in kalam: every mukallaf (legally responsible person) must know the three rational categories (al-ahkam al-'aqliyya) — wajib (necessary), ja'iz (possible), and mumtani' (impossible) — as they apply to Allah and to His messengers. The poet emphasizes 'wajaba' (it is necessarily incumbent) and urges the reader to listen (ista'mi'a).

Key Terms
كلف شرعا
وجب
عرف
واجب
جائز
ممتنع
رسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only scholars or advanced students need to learn the three categories.
Correction: The poet declares it an obligation upon every mukallaf, not just scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, who is obligated to know the three rational categories?
A) Only scholars
B) Every mukallaf
C) Only prophets
D) Only men

Key Takeaway
Every mukallaf must know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah and His messengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do I personally know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah? Have I ever taken the time to learn these categories, or have I neglected this obligation while focusing on less essential matters?
?
When I think about the prophets, do I have correct beliefs about what they can and cannot do? Am I aware that attributing lying or betrayal to a prophet contradicts what Allah has made impossible for them?
?
This lesson says knowing these categories is wajib on me as a mukallaf. Have I ever felt accountable for my beliefs about Allah and His messengers? How will I ensure that I fulfill this duty starting today?

Contemplating Creation as a Sign of Contingency
The poet commands reflection on creation as evidence of contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the command to reflect on self and universe reveals the inherent contingency of creation.

Core Idea
Contingency is stamped upon everything we see.

The poet begins his proof by turning our attention to the most accessible evidence: ourselves, the heavens, and the earth. He urges us to observe the exquisite design present in all things. But this observation is not an end in itself—it is the foundation for a deeper logical move. In this lesson, we focus on the initial call to contemplation and the recognition of stunning craftsmanship.

Guided Path
1
Read the first line of the passage
Read the Arabic line: فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي تجد به صنعًا بديع الحكم. Then read its translation.
Notice the sequence: self, upper world, lower world.
2
Identify the three realms of reflection
The poet lists three realms: yourself (النفس), the upper world (العالم العلوي), and the lower world (العالم السفلي). Why do you think he starts with yourself?
This shows the proof is empirical and personal.
3
Recognize the description of design
The poet says you will find 'صنعًا بديع الحكم'—a wonderful craftsmanship. This implies order, purpose, and wisdom. But note the word 'بديع' suggests innovativeness, pointing to a Creator.
The design is not random; it is 'wonderful' and 'wise'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي تجد به صنعًا بديع الحكم لكن به قام دليل العدم. وكل ما جاز عليه العدم عليه قطعًا يستحيل القدم.
Look at yourself, then move to the upper world and then the lower; you will find in it a wonderful craftsmanship, but through it the proof of non-existence stands. And everything upon which non-existence is possible, upon it perpetuity is necessarily impossible.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on the observation of contingent beings. The poet begins by directing the reader to reflect on oneself, then on the celestial and terrestrial realms. One finds in them a remarkable design, but this very contingency points to the possibility of their non-existence—hence they are not eternal. The conclusion is that anything that can cease to exist cannot be eternal (qadim). Thus, only God, who is necessary, is eternal.

Key Terms
صنع بديع
الحكم
نفس
عالم علوي
عالم سفلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the poet command the reader to do first?
A) Reflect on the upper world only
B) Look at yourself
C) Look at the lower world only
D) Ignore self and look at the cosmos

Key Takeaway
The poet's proof begins with a call to observe the intricate design in oneself and the cosmos, setting the stage for the contingency argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at yourself, do you truly see every detail as a sign of God's wisdom, or have you become numb to the miracle of your own existence? What would change if you started every day with the poet's command: 'Look at yourself'?
?
You have observed the vastness of the upper world and the intricacy of the lower world; have you ever paused long enough to feel the helplessness of their contingency? When will you stop treating creation as self-sufficient and admit your need for the Creator?
?
The poet says you will find 'wonderful craftsmanship' — but are you merely admiring it, or are you using it to build a relationship with the Wise Maker? What step will you take today to let that observation lead you to sincere submission?

The Logical Conclusion: Contingent Cannot Be Eternal
The poet states the logical rule linking contingency to impossibility of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inference from contingency to the impossibility of eternity for contingent beings.

Core Idea
What can cease to exist cannot have existed eternally.

Having observed the remarkable design of the universe, the poet now draws the crucial logical conclusion. The very contingency of creation—the fact that its existence is not necessary—proves that it cannot be eternal. This paves the way for affirming the existence of a Necessary Being who alone is eternal. In this lesson, we unpack the final line of the passage and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second line of the passage
Read the Arabic: لكن به قام دليل العدم. وكل ما جاز عليه العدم عليه قطعًا يستحيل القدم. Then read its translation.
This is a universal statement: everything possible of non-existence is impossible of perpetuity.
2
Understand the terms: العدم and القدم
العدم means non-existence. القدم means perpetuity or eternity without beginning. The poet claims that if non-existence is possible for something, then that thing cannot be eternal.
This is a logical principle: what is contingent cannot be necessary.
3
Apply the principle to creation
Since the design we observed in lesson1 is contingent (it could have been otherwise or not existed at all), it follows that the universe is not eternal. Therefore, it must have a Creator who is eternal.
This is the core of the contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي تجد به صنعًا بديع الحكم لكن به قام دليل العدم. وكل ما جاز عليه العدم عليه قطعًا يستحيل القدم.
Look at yourself, then move to the upper world and then the lower; you will find in it a wonderful craftsmanship, but through it the proof of non-existence stands. And everything upon which non-existence is possible, upon it perpetuity is necessarily impossible.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of God based on the observation of contingent beings. The poet begins by directing the reader to reflect on oneself, then on the celestial and terrestrial realms. One finds in them a remarkable design, but this very contingency points to the possibility of their non-existence—hence they are not eternal. The conclusion is that anything that can cease to exist cannot be eternal (qadim). Thus, only God, who is necessary, is eternal.

Key Terms
العدم
القدم
جاز
يستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the contingency argument is a form of design argument that stops at the observation of complexity.
Correction: The contingency argument goes further: it uses the logical impossibility of a contingent being being eternal, thus requiring a Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
According to the poet, what is the relationship between contingency (possibility of non-existence) and eternity?
A) Contingent beings can be eternal if they have a cause
B) If something can cease to exist, it cannot be eternal
C) Eternity is possible for all beings
D) Only the Creator is contingent

Key Takeaway
The contingency argument logically concludes that nothing contingent can be eternal, pointing to the necessity of an eternal Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything contingent cannot be eternal, then your own existence is contingent. Have you ever truly internalized that you are not self-sufficient, that your existence hangs on the will of the Eternal? How does that change the way you live today?
?
The universe's design points to its contingency, and contingency demands a Creator. You have seen this proof. But do you live as though you really believe? Do your actions reflect that you are accountable to the Eternal, or do you act as if this world is all there is?
?
This passage forces a logical conclusion: what can cease to exist cannot have always existed. Your life will cease. Are you living for what is eternal or what is passing? What one decision can you make today to align with the truth of this lesson?

Thing, Existence, and the Atom
Sanusi defines 'thing' as existent and affirms the created atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Ash'ari theology defines 'thing' as existent in external reality, identifies existence with essence (for God), and affirms the createdness of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
In Ash'ari kalam, 'thing' means real existent, and the atom is created.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin introduces three foundational ontological claims in Ash'ari theology. First, it defines what a 'thing' truly is. Then it clarifies the relationship between existence and essence. Finally, it addresses the nature of the indivisible atom, a key concept in kalam cosmology. These points establish the basic framework for discussing creation and God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the text makes three distinct claims in a single sentence.
The passage is dense; each claim deserves reflection.
2
Understand the definition of 'thing'
Identify the first claim: 'الشَّيء هو الموجود وثابت في الخارج'. This defines a thing as something that exists in external reality, not merely in the mind.
This rejects pure mental existence as sufficient for being a 'thing'.
3
Examine the identity of existence and essence
The phrase 'وجودُ شَيءٍ عينه' states that for a thing, its existence is identical to its essence. In Ash'ari theology, this applies primarily to God, but the text presents it as a general ontological claim.
Be careful not to misinterpret this as pantheism.
4
Learn about the indivisible atom
The final claim: 'الجَوهَرُ الفرد حادث عندنا لا يُنْكَرُ'. The indivisible atom (jawhar fard) is originated (hadith), meaning it is created and not eternal.
This atom is the basic building block of matter in kalam cosmology.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi start with these definitions? They establish that the world consists of real, created entities, setting the stage for arguments about God's existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا الشَّيء هو الموجود وثابت في الخارج الموجود وجودُ شَيءٍ عينه والجَوهَرُ الفرد حادث عندنا لا يُنْكَرُ
And according to us, the thing is the existent, and fixed in the external, existent. The existence of a thing is its essence, and the indivisible atom is originated according to us, not denied.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents ontological claims. First, it defines 'thing' (shay') as that which is existent (mawjud) and established in external reality (thabit fi al-kharij). This rejects the notion that mental concepts alone are things. Then it states that the existence of a thing is its essence (wujud shay' 'aynahu), which in Ash'ari theology applies primarily to God (identity of essence and existence) but may also be understood in a broader sense. Finally, it affirms the origination (hadath) of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), a key concept in kalam cosmology, asserting that it is created and not eternal.

Key Terms
الشَّيء (thing)
الموجود (the existent)
الخارج (external reality)
وجودُ شَيءٍ عينه (existence of a thing is its essence)
الجَوهَرُ الفرد (indivisible atom)
حادث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'existence of a thing is its essence' might be understood as pantheism (everything is God).
Correction: In context, this affirms that for created things, their existence is real and not an accident separate from their essence. For God, existence and essence are identical, but for creatures they are distinct in concept, though the text presents the identity as a general ontological claim.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the status of the indivisible atom?
A) It is eternal and uncreated
B) It is originated (hadith)
C) It does not exist
D) It is only a mental concept

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school affirms that things truly exist in external reality, that existence is intrinsic to essence, and that the indivisible atom is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the smallest particle you can imagine, do you truly believe it was brought into existence by God at a specific moment? How does that certainty shape your view of the universe's utter dependence on Him right now?
?
The passage says that the existence of a thing is its essence. Does your own sense of existence feel like an independent attribute, or do you recognize that you only exist because God continuously gives you existence?
?
If the atom is created, then everything you touch, see, and rely on is a direct act of creation. How would your daily gratitude change if you remembered this every time you used a physical object?

The Source of Obligation: Reason or Revelation?
Al-Laqqani explains the Ash'ari position on the source of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the obligation to know God is established by divine law, not by reason alone, and how this shapes the concept of rational judgment in kalam.

Core Idea
Obligation to know God comes from revelation, not reason.

This passage from al-Laqqani's Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a foundational point of Ash'ari theology: the source of obligation. Before discussing what we must believe, we must first ask where the obligation to believe comes from. Is it from reason or from revelation? Al-Laqqani presents the Ash'ari answer and distinguishes it from the Mu'tazilite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the grammatical note about 'shar'an' being placed before the verb to indicate restriction.
The passage begins with an orientation: 'An explanation of the divisions of rational judgment.'
2
Identify the key grammatical point
Al-Laqqani explains that 'shar'an' (by law) is accusative and placed before the verb to indicate restriction (hasr). This emphasizes that obligation is specifically by law, not by reason.
The author refutes an alternative interpretation that would change the meaning.
3
Understand the Ash'ari position
The passage states the Ash'ari school: knowledge of Allah and all rulings are obligatory only by the command of divine law. Reason alone does not impose obligation; it discovers what revelation commands.
This contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view that reason can determine obligation independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي، وما يجب معرفته في حق الله وحقِّ الرُّسُل] وقوله: (شرعاً) منصوب بنزع الخافض أي بالشَّرع، متعلق بـ (وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ) لكنَّه قدَّمه لإفادة الحصر، والمعنى: لا يجب على المكلَّف أَنْ [؟] من الناس من جعل الجار والمجرور متعلقا بقوله : «كلف»، وليس بشيء؛ لأن الغرض بيان أنَّ معرفة الله تعالى وجبت على المكلف بإيجاب الشرع، وليس وجوبها بالعقل، وليس المقصود بيان أن التكليف حاصل بالشرع. هذا الذي ذكرناه من أنَّ المعرفة وجبت بإيجاب الشرع لا بالعقل، هو مذهب الأشاعرة، وجمع من غيرهم، وحاصل مذهبهم: أن معرفة الله تعالى، وكذلك سائر الأحكام إنَّما وجبت بإيجاب الشرع، وأنه لا حكم قبل الشرع لا أصليا ولا فرعيا.
An explanation of the divisions of rational judgment, and what must be known concerning Allah and the Messengers. And His statement: 'by law' (shar'an) is in the accusative due to the removal of the preposition, meaning 'by the law' (al-shar'), attached to 'obligatory upon him' (wajaba 'alayhi), but he advanced it to indicate restriction. And the meaning is: it is not obligatory upon the morally responsible person to... Some people make the prepositional phrase attached to His statement: 'charged' (kallafa), and it is nothing, because the purpose is to explain that knowledge of Allah, Exalted, is obligatory upon the morally responsible person by the command of the law, and its obligation is not by reason, and the purpose is not to explain that accountability is obtained by the law. This that we have mentioned, that knowledge is obligatory by the command of the law, not by reason, is the school of the Ash'aris and a group of others. And the summary of their school: that knowledge of Allah, Exalted, as well as all other rulings, are only obligatory by the command of the law, and that there is no ruling before the law, neither fundamental nor derived.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the nature of rational judgment (hukm 'aqli) and specifically addresses the obligation to know God. It begins by stating the topic: 'an explanation of the divisions of rational judgment, and what must be known concerning Allah and the Messengers.' The author then comments on a phrase from the original text: 'by law' (shar'an). He explains that it is in the accusative because the preposition 'bi' (by) has been omitted, so it means 'by the law' (al-shar'). This phrase is attached to the statement 'obligatory upon him' (wajaba 'alayhi) from the preceding line, but it is placed before it to indicate restriction (hasr), emphasizing that the obligation is specifically by law. The intended meaning is that it is not obligatory upon the morally responsible person to know God except by revelation. The author then refutes an alternative interpretation that attaches the phrase to the word 'charged' (kallafa), saying that is incorrect because the purpose is to show that the obligation itself is from the law, not that accountability is from the law. He then summarizes the Ash'ari position: the obligation to know God and all other rulings are obligatory only by the command of divine law (shar'), and there is no ruling before the law, neither fundamental nor derived. This contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view that reason alone can determine obligation. The passage thus sets the foundation for the 'divisions of rational judgment' by clarifying that rational judgments are defined by revelation, not independent reason.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
شرع
وجوب
عقل
أشاعرة
معتزلة
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'rational judgment' means reason alone determines obligation, and that reason can independently impose duties.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that all obligations come only from divine law; reason discovers the law but does not create obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position as stated in the passage, how are obligations such as knowing God established?
A) Through rational deduction alone.
B) Only through divine law (shar').
C) Through consensus of scholars.
D) Through personal experience.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to know God and all other rulings are only obligatory by the command of divine law, not by reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on your own reasoning to decide what is right or wrong, without seeking guidance from revelation? How does this lesson challenge you to submit even your moral judgments to the law?
?
When you think about your knowledge of Allah, do you feel it is primarily a product of your own rational efforts, or a gift received through revelation? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize divine guidance over your own intellect?
?
This lesson teaches that there is no obligation before the law. Is there any area of your life where you have assumed a moral duty that you have not anchored in the Qur’an and Sunnah? What would it cost you to align that area with revelation?

intermediate
5 min
The Mu'tazili View on Rational Obligation
Explains the Mu'tazili doctrine of rational obligation and its basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili position that reason alone establishes all moral and theological judgments, including knowledge of God, and that revelation merely confirms reason.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites hold that reason independently determines good and evil and obligates knowledge of God.

This lesson focuses on the first of the three major schools presented in the passage: the Mu'tazila. They are known for their strong emphasis on rational judgment (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-'aqliyyān), asserting that reason can independently determine what is good and evil and consequently establish obligations. The passage explains their core doctrine and how revelation fits into their framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms 'العقل' and 'الشرع' and how they are contrasted.
The passage begins by stating the Mu'tazili view in the context of rational judgment.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili claims
Identify the two main claims: (1) all judgments are established by reason, and (2) revelation only confirms what reason already determined.
Note the phrase 'بناء على مذهبهم في التحسين والتقبيح العقليين' which grounds their view.
3
Understand the concept of rational approval/disapproval
Understand that for the Mu'tazila, 'good' is what reason sees as good, and 'evil' is what reason sees as evil. This leads to the obligation to act accordingly.
This concept is foundational to their epistemology.
4
Contrast with the Ash'ari position (preview)
Notice that the Mu'tazili view is opposed to the Ash'ari position (to be covered later). The passage sets up this contrast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي يَعْرِفَ أي: معرفة ما قد وجبا لله عقلا إلا بالشرع؛ إذ قبله لا حكم، وأن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل. وأما الشرع فإنما جاء مقررًا ومؤكدًا لما أثبته العقل، وهذا بناء على مذهبهم في التحسين والتقبيح العقليين؛ فعندهم أن الحسن هو ما رآه العقل حسنًا، والقبيح هو ما رآه قبيحًا، وعندهم أنه إذا أدرك العقل حسن شيء حكم بوجوبه، ويجب أن يجيء الشرع فيه مطابقًا لما حكم به العقل. وذهب أبو منصور الماتريدي ومن شايعه إلى أن معرفة الله تعالى وحدها يوجبها العقل، لكن على معنى أنه لو لم يرد الشرع لأدرك العقل ذلك استقلالاً لكونه أمرًا واضحًا، ولم يبنوا ذلك على التحسين العقلي كما فعل المعتزلة؛ فالمذاهب في مسألة المعرفة ثلاثة: الأول مذهب الأشاعرة، وحاصله: أن جميع الأحكام ومنها معرفة الله تعالى إنما تثبت بالشرع، ويكلف بها العقلاء. والثاني مذهب أبي منصور الماتريدي، وحاصله: أن المعرفة وحدها تثبت بالعقل، وأما سائر الأحكام فلا تثبت إلا بالشرع. والثالث مذهب المعتزلة، وحاصله: أن الأحكام كلها ـ ومنها معرفة الله تعالى ـ ثبتت بالعقل، وجاء الشرع مبينًا ومؤكدًا لما أثبته العقل. ومما قررناه لك في بيان هذه المذاهب الثلاثة تفهم أن الفرق بين مذهب الماتريدية والمعتزلة من جهتين: الأولى: أن الماتريدية خصوا ما أثبته العقل بالمعرفة، وأما المعتزلة فجعلوا ما يثبته العقل عامًا في كل الأحكام. الثانية: أن معنى إثبات العقل لوجوب المعرفة عند الماتريدية أنه يستطيع حسن الاستقلال بإدراكه لكونه واضحًا جليًا، ومعنى ذلك عند المعتزلة أنه رأى أن وجوب المعرفة حسن فحكم بثبوته، وفرق بين المعنيين. وإذا علمت هذا كله تبين لك أن قول المصنف والشارح "شَرعًا" رد على طائفتين من المتكلمين، وهما المعتزلة والماتريدية.
Clarification of the divisions of rational judgment: He knows, i.e., knowledge of what is necessary for God, by reason, except through the Law; because before it there is no judgment, and that all judgments are established by reason. As for the Law, it only comes as a confirmer and reaffirmer of what reason has established, and this is based on their doctrine of rational approval and disapproval (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ al-'aqliyyayn). For according to them, the good is what reason sees as good, and the evil is what reason sees as evil, and according to them, when reason perceives the goodness of something, it judges its obligation, and the Law must come in accordance with what reason has judged. Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and those who followed him held that knowledge of God Almighty alone is necessitated by reason, but in the sense that if the Law had not come, reason would have grasped it independently because it is a clear matter, and they did not base that on rational approval as the Mu'tazila did. So the schools on the issue of knowledge are three: First, the Ash'ari school, its gist: that all judgments, including knowledge of God Almighty, are only established by the Law, and rational beings are charged with them. Second, the school of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, its gist: that knowledge alone is established by reason, but the rest of the judgments are only established by the Law. Third, the Mu'tazili school, its gist: that all judgments—including knowledge of God Almighty—are established by reason, and the Law comes as an elucidator and reaffirmer of what reason has established. From what we have set out for you in the clarification of these three schools, you understand that the difference between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools is from two aspects: First: that the Maturidis restricted what reason establishes to knowledge, while the Mu'tazilis made what reason establish general to all judgments. Second: that the meaning of reason's establishment of the obligation of knowledge according to the Maturidis is that it is capable of good independence in perceiving it because it is clear and evident; and its meaning according to the Mu'tazilis is that it saw that the obligation of knowledge is good, so it judged its establishment, and there is a difference between the two meanings. When you know all this, it becomes clear to you that the statement of the author and the commentator "by the Law" is a refutation of two groups of theologians, namely the Mu'tazila and the Maturidiyya.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the three major schools of Islamic theology regarding the role of reason in establishing obligations, particularly the obligation to know God. It begins by stating that knowledge of what is necessary for God is obtained through the Law, because before revelation there is no judgment. Then it explains the Ash'ari position: all judgments (including knowledge of God) are established by the Law, although reason confirms them. This is based on the doctrine of rational approval and disapproval (at-taḥsīn wa at-taqbīḥ al-'aqliyyayn). Next, it presents the Maturidi position: only knowledge of God is established by reason independently because it is clear; other obligations require the Law. It distinguishes this from the Mu'tazili position, which holds that all judgments are established by reason, and the Law merely confirms them. The passage then highlights two specific differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazilis: (1) scope—Maturidis limit rational establishment to knowledge, Mu'tazilis extend it to all judgments; (2) mechanism—Maturidis say reason perceives clarity, Mu'tazilis say reason judges good and thus obligation. Finally, it concludes that the author's and commentator's use of 'by the Law' refutes both the Mu'tazila and Maturidiyya (who affirm some rational obligation).

Key Terms
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
العقل
الشرع
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili view as presented in the passage, what role does revelation play?
A) Revelation establishes all judgments independently of reason.
B) Revelation comes as a confirmer and reaffirmer of what reason has established.
C) Revelation provides the only source of moral knowledge.
D) Revelation is unnecessary because reason is sufficient for all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila affirm that reason independently establishes moral and theological obligations, with revelation serving only to confirm reason's findings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you rely more on your rational judgment or on revealed texts? How does this Mu'tazili claim challenge your default approach?
?
If reason alone can know God's obligations, why do you still need revelation? Has this lesson exposed a hidden reliance on reason over scripture in your own thinking?
?
Are you willing to submit your rational conclusions to revelation if they conflict, or do you find yourself rationalizing revelation to fit your reason? Reflect on a specific instance where they clashed.

Rational Judgments

5 min
The Maturidi View on Rational Knowledge of God
Explains the Maturidi position on rational knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that reason alone can establish the obligation to know God, but only because it is self-evident, and that other obligations require revelation.

Core Idea
Maturidis affirm rational obligation only for knowledge of God, not for all judgments.

This lesson examines the Maturidi school, which takes a middle position between the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris. The passage explains that for the Maturidis, reason can independently establish the obligation to know God because this is a clear and self-evident matter. However, they do not extend this to all judgments, unlike the Mu'tazila. The lesson also clarifies the basis of their view, which avoids the rational approval/disapproval framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage, focusing on the Maturidi claim about knowledge of God and the contrast with the Mu'tazila.
Note the phrase 'لم يبنوا ذلك على التحسين العقلي كما فعل المعتزلة' which highlights a key difference.
2
Identify the scope of rational obligation
Identify that the Maturidis limit rational obligation to knowledge of God alone ('معرفة الله تعالى وحدها يوجبها العقل').
The passage later reiterates this in the summary of the second school.
3
Understand the mechanism
Understand that for the Maturidis, reason knows this obligation because the matter is clear and evident, not because it judges something as 'good' in the Mu'tazili sense.
This is a subtle but important distinction.
4
Preview the two differences
Note that the passage will later list two specific differences between the Maturidis and Mu'tazila, one of which is this scope, and the other the mechanism.
This lesson only covers the Maturidi position itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, what is the basis for reason's obligation to know God?
A) Reason judges that knowing God is good, so it is obligatory.
B) The matter is clear and evident, so reason grasps it independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you test your belief in God, is it based on rational certainty that you could defend without any external source, or is it merely inherited? The Maturidis claim that knowing God is self-evident to reason. Have you ever truly experienced that self-evidence in your own mind? If not, what does that say about your reliance on revelation?
?
If you were stripped of all religious teachings and scriptures, and left only with your pure intellect, would you still affirm the existence of God? What would your answer reveal about the foundation of your faith?
?
Are you content to believe in God because of tradition and evidence from revelation, or have you personally grasped the rational necessity that the Maturidis describe? Your answer determines whether your faith is built on reason or on authority. Which do you base your life on?

Module 10 · Lesson 9

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
The Ash'ari View and the Two Differences
Summarizes the Ash'ari view and lists two differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazilis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that all judgments, including knowledge of God, are established only by revelation, and the two key differences between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools regarding rational obligation.

Core Idea
Ash'aris deny any rational obligation; Maturidis and Mu'tazilis differ on scope and basis.

This lesson covers the Ash'ari school and then clarifies the differences between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools as outlined in the passage. The Ash'aris hold that reason alone cannot establish any obligations; all come through revelation. The passage then contrasts the two reason-affirming schools by noting that the Maturidis limit rational establishment to knowledge of God (scope) and base it on clarity, while the Mu'tazilis extend it to all judgments and base it on rational approval of goodness (mechanism). Finally, the passage concludes that the author's use of 'by the Law' refutes both the Mu'tazila and Maturidiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the entire passage for this lesson, which includes the Ash'ari summary and the two differences.
Note that the Ash'ari view is stated very concisely.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position
Identify that for Ash'aris, all judgments including knowledge of God are established only by the Law.
The phrase 'جميع الأحكام ومنها معرفة الله تعالى إنما تثبت بالشرع' is key.
3
Grasp the two differences
Understand the two differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazilis: (1) scope (knowledge only vs. all judgments), and (2) mechanism (clarity vs. rational approval).
These differences are explicitly numbered in the passage.
4
Understand the refutation
Understand that by stating 'شَرعًا' (by the Law), the author refutes both the Mu'tazila and Maturidiyya, since both affirm some rational obligation.
This is the conclusion and shows the Ash'ari stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي يَعْرِفَ أي: معرفة ما قد وجبا لله عقلا إلا بالشرع؛ إذ قبله لا حكم، وأن الأحكام كلها ثبتت بالعقل. وأما الشرع فإنما جاء مقررًا ومؤكدًا لما أثبته العقل، وهذا بناء على مذهبهم في التحسين والتقبيح العقليين؛ فعندهم أن الحسن هو ما رآه العقل حسنًا، والقبيح هو ما رآه قبيحًا، وعندهم أنه إذا أدرك العقل حسن شيء حكم بوجوبه، ويجب أن يجيء الشرع فيه مطابقًا لما حكم به العقل. وذهب أبو منصور الماتريدي ومن شايعه إلى أن معرفة الله تعالى وحدها يوجبها العقل، لكن على معنى أنه لو لم يرد الشرع لأدرك العقل ذلك استقلالاً لكونه أمرًا واضحًا، ولم يبنوا ذلك على التحسين العقلي كما فعل المعتزلة؛ فالمذاهب في مسألة المعرفة ثلاثة: الأول مذهب الأشاعرة، وحاصله: أن جميع الأحكام ومنها معرفة الله تعالى إنما تثبت بالشرع، ويكلف بها العقلاء. والثاني مذهب أبي منصور الماتريدي، وحاصله: أن المعرفة وحدها تثبت بالعقل، وأما سائر الأحكام فلا تثبت إلا بالشرع. والثالث مذهب المعتزلة، وحاصله: أن الأحكام كلها ـ ومنها معرفة الله تعالى ـ ثبتت بالعقل، وجاء الشرع مبينًا ومؤكدًا لما أثبته العقل. ومما قررناه لك في بيان هذه المذاهب الثلاثة تفهم أن الفرق بين مذهب الماتريدية والمعتزلة من جهتين: الأولى: أن الماتريدية خصوا ما أثبته العقل بالمعرفة، وأما المعتزلة فجعلوا ما يثبته العقل عامًا في كل الأحكام. الثانية: أن معنى إثبات العقل لوجوب المعرفة عند الماتريدية أنه يستطيع حسن الاستقلال بإدراكه لكونه واضحًا جليًا، ومعنى ذلك عند المعتزلة أنه رأى أن وجوب المعرفة حسن فحكم بثبوته، وفرق بين المعنيين. وإذا علمت هذا كله تبين لك أن قول المصنف والشارح "شَرعًا" رد على طائفتين من المتكلمين، وهما المعتزلة والماتريدية.

Next, it presents the Maturidi position: only knowledge of God is established by reason independently because it is clear; other obligations require the Law. It distinguishes this from the Mu'tazili position, which holds that all judgments are established by reason, and the Law merely confirms them. The passage then highlights two specific differences between Maturidis and Mu'tazilis: (1) scope—Maturidis limit rational establishment to knowledge, Mu'tazilis extend it to all judgments; (2) mechanism—Maturidis say reason perceives clarity, Mu'tazilis say reason judges good and thus obligation. Finally, it concludes that the author's and commentator's use of 'by the Law' refutes both the Mu'tazila and Maturidiyya (who affirm some rational obligation).

Key Terms
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
المعتزلة
المعرفة
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three schools agree that reason plays the same role in establishing obligations.
Correction: They differ significantly: Ash'aris deny any rational obligation, Maturidis affirm it only for knowledge of God, and Mu'tazilis affirm it for all judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second difference between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools regarding rational obligation for knowledge?
A) Maturidis limit rational establishment to knowledge, while Mu'tazilis extend it to all judgments.
B) Maturidis say reason perceives the clarity of the obligation, while Mu'tazilis say reason judges it as good.
C) Maturidis say reason is insufficient, while Mu'tazilis say reason is the sole source.
D) Maturidis follow Ash'aris, while Mu'tazilis follow philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school denies any rational obligation, while the Maturidi and Mu'tazili schools differ in scope and basis, a distinction the passage uses to refute both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Ash'aris deny any rational obligation to know God, does your heart feel a tension between what reason tells you you owe God and what revelation commands? Where in your life do you actually rely on reason versus revelation for your most important duties?
?
The Mu'tazilis say the Law only confirms what reason already sees as good. Examine yourself: how often do you obey a divine command simply because it is commanded, rather than because you personally approve of it? Which school does your actual practice follow?
?
The passage says that according to the Maturidis, reason can independently perceive the obligation to know God because it is 'clear and evident.' If that is true, why do so many people live without any sense of that obligation? What does your own negligence in seeking knowledge of God reveal about your view of reason's capacity?

Rational Judgments

The Three Rational Judgments and the Obligation of Knowledge
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and argues that knowledge of them is obligatory, even with a general proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (possible, obligatory, impossible) and the obligation to know them with at least a general proof, moving from imitation to verification.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the foundation of Islamic theology; knowing them with a general proof suffices to avoid blind imitation.

This lesson establishes the conceptual framework of rational judgments in Islamic theology. The author defines three categories—possible, obligatory, and impossible—and asserts that even a general proof is sufficient to move from imitation to verification. Understanding these categories is essential for sound theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three judgments
The passage mentions three rational judgments: possible (jāʾiz), impossible (mumtaniʿ), and obligatory (wājib). Notice how each relates to God.
The possible and impossible are introduced as 'as well' (كذلك), implying they are defined analogously.
2
Understand the requirement of a general proof
The author states that even a general proof (dalīl jumlī) suffices to exit taqlīd and achieve verification.
A general proof is not a detailed one, but it is sufficient for obligation.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
Three proofs are given: Quran verse 'faʿlamannahu lā ilāha illā Allāh', hadith about fighting until testimony, and consensus.
These establish the obligation to know God's oneness.
4
Define the obligatory
The obligatory is defined as that which the intellect cannot conceive of its non-existence. Example: a body must occupy space.
This definition applies to necessary truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالجَائِزَ في حقه الله كذلك ، (والمُمْتَنِعَا) عليه كذلك ، ولو بدليل جُمْلي (١) ، يخرج به المُكلَّفُ مِن التَّقليد إلى التحقيق ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ﴾ [محمد: ۱۹] وحديثِ: «أُمِرْتُ أَنْ أُقاتِلَ النَّاسَ حتَّى يَشهدوا أَنْ لا إله إِلَّا الله» (۲) وللإجماع على ذلك (۳). يجب (٤) والواجب : ما لا يُتَصوَّرُ في العقل (4) عدمه ، ضرورةً: كالتَّحيُّز للجِرْمِ
And the possible in His right God likewise, and the impossible in His right likewise, even with a general proof (1), by which the responsible person exits from imitation to verification; due to His statement Most High: 'So know that there is no god except Allah' (Muhammad 19), and the hadith: 'I have been commanded to fight people until they testify that there is no god except Allah' (2), and due to consensus on that (3). It is obligatory (4) and the obligatory is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect (4) necessarily, such as occupying space for a body.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the three rational judgments (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī): the possible (jāʾiz), the obligatory (wājib), and the impossible (mumtaniʿ). It states that one must know these judgments regarding God, even with a general proof (dalīl jumlī), which suffices to move from blind imitation (taqlīd) to verification (taḥqīq). Evidence is cited from the Quran (verse 'faʿlamannahu lā ilāha illā Allāh'), the hadith on fighting until the testimony, and scholarly consensus. Then the obligatory is defined as that which the intellect cannot conceive of its non-existence, e.g., a body necessarily occupying space.

Correction: The passage clearly states that even a general proof suffices to exit taqlīd and fulfill the obligation of knowledge.

Rational Judgments
beginner

The Three Rational Judgments: Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
Defines three rational judgments with examples and subtypes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) and their subdivisions: by necessity (darura) and by reasoning (nazar).

Core Idea
Rational judgments are the logical categories for all theological claims.

Sanusi establishes the foundational taxonomy of rational judgments that every student of kalam must grasp. Before discussing any theological proposition, we must know whether it is rationally necessary, impossible, or possible. This passage defines each category with immediate and reasoned examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the pattern: each judgment is defined and then split into two types.
2
Identify the three categories and their subtypes
Find the definitions of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz. For each, note the two subtypes: darura (by necessity) and nazar (by reasoning). Pay attention to the examples given.
Darura examples are immediate, nazar examples require inference.
3
Study the unified example
The passage uses motion and rest of a body to illustrate all three judgments. Work through how the same example applies to wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz.
This helps see how the categories interrelate.
4
Reflect on the purpose
The final sentence states the aim: to know all particulars through these universal categories, even if only by a general principle. Consider why this is important in kalam.
The categories are tools for reasoning about all theological matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والواجب : ما لا يُتَصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه ، ضرورةً: كالتَّحيُّز للجِرْمِ، أو نظرًا (۱) : كوجوب القدم له تعالى . والمستحيل : ما لا يتصوّر في العقل وجوده ، ضرورةً : كتَعرّي الجرم عن الحركة والسّكون (۲) ، أو نظراً: كالشّريك له تعالى . والجائز: ما يصح في نظر العقل وجوده وعدمه، ضرورةً: كالحركة أو السكون للجرم (۳) ، أو نظراً : كتعذيب المطيع وإثابة العاصي. ويُمثَلُ للثّلاثة أقسام : بحركة الجرم وسكونه ; فالواجب: ثبوت أحدهما لا بعينه والمستحيل خُلُوُّه عنهما جميعاً ، والجائز: ثبوت أحدهما له مُعيَّناً ، بدلًا من الآخر. والمراد: معرفة جميع جزئيات هذه الكليات حسب الطاقة البشرية ، ولو بقانون كلي (٤).
The wajib (necessary): that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect, by necessity (darura): like matter's [؟] occupation of space for the atom [sic]; or by reasoning (nazar): like the necessity of pre-eternity for Him, exalted. The mustahil (impossible): that whose existence is inconceivable in the intellect, by necessity: like a body being devoid of motion and rest; or by reasoning: like a partner to Him, exalted. The ja'iz (possible): that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the consideration of the intellect, by necessity: like motion or rest for a body; or by reasoning: like punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient. And an example is given for the three divisions by the motion and rest of a body: so the wajib is the affirmation of one of them not specifically, the mustahil is its being devoid of both, and the ja'iz is the affirmation of one of them for it specifically instead of the other. And the intent is the knowledge of all particulars of these universals according to human capacity, even by a universal principle.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It first defines the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) each subdivided into two types: darura (immediate, necessary for the intellect) and nazar (by reasoning, through inference). For wajib, the darura example is a body's occupation of space, and the nazar example is God's pre-eternity. For mustahil, the darura example is a body being devoid of both motion and rest, and the nazar example is a partner to God. For ja'iz, the darura example is motion or rest for a body, and the nazar example is punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient (since God may do so but is not necessitated). Then it gives a unified example using a body's motion and rest: it is wajib that the body has one of them (not specifically), mustahil to lack both, and ja'iz to have one specifically. Finally, it states that the purpose is to know all particulars of these universal categories within human capacity, even if only through a general principle (qanun kulli). The passage covers the foundational taxonomy of rational judgments used in kalam.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
ضرورة
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'wajib by necessity' applies only to abstract logical truths, not to physical entities.
Correction: The passage gives the example of a body occupying space as wajib by darura, showing that the category applies to physical realities as well.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a wajib by nazar (reasoning)?
A) A body's occupation of space
B) The necessity of God's pre-eternity
C) A body being devoid of motion and rest
D) Punishing the obedient

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) are the building blocks of all theological reasoning, each with immediate and reasoned applications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something is impossible for God based on your own limited reasoning, even though it is only ja'iz? How does this lesson challenge you to submit your mind to the categories of necessary, impossible, and possible as defined by the scholars?

When you reflect on your own belief in God, is it based on immediate certainty or inferential reasoning? Which subtype of wajib (darura or nazar) better describes your conviction, and what does that reveal about your need to either strengthen or verify your faith?

The passage teaches that we can know all particulars through a general principle. Are there areas of your faith where you have not applied this principle—where you have doubted a specific truth because you could not see its detailed proof? How will you apply the general principle to trust God’s wisdom today?

Obligation of All Mukallaf to Know Creedal Matters
Sanusi explains who is obligated and the conditions for using evidence vs. imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all mukallaf, including common people, slaves, women, and servants, are obligated to know the creedal matters through evidence if they have capacity, otherwise imitation suffices.

Core Idea
Every responsible person must know basic beliefs, but capacity determines whether they need proofs or can follow scholars.

This lesson addresses a common misconception that only scholars are obligated to study theology. The passage explicitly includes all categories of mukallaf and clarifies the role of evidence and imitation.

Guided Path
1
Identify who is included in mukallaf
Read the list: common people, slaves, women, servants. All are obligated.
Note that this includes everyone with intellect.
2
Understand the condition of capacity
The phrase 'mata kana fihim ahliyya' means if they have the ability to understand evidence.
Al-Sanusi argued capacity is universal.
3
Recognize the alternative: taqlid
If lacking capacity, imitation is sufficient.
This shows Islam's practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي
•
ودخل في المكلَّف: العوام، والعبيد، والنِّسوان، والخَدَمُ؛ فإنَّهم مكلَّفونَ بمعرفة العقائد عن الأدلة، متى كان فيهم أهليَّة فهمها (1)، وإلا كفاهم التقليد.
(1) قوله: متى كان فيهم أهلية فهمها ردَّ ذلك الإمام السنوسي في «شرح الوسطى» بقوله: إن الأهلية في غاية الندور، أو ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنظر. اهـ :أي: لأن المطلوب الدليل الجملي، وهو متيسر من كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف. الشنواني.
عدم
(2) أشار المصنف بلفظ: «مثل» إلى أنّ الواجب في حقّ الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، والمستحيل والجائز ليست هي عين الواجب في حقه، والجائز والمستحيل، فالمراد المثلية في مطلق واجب ومستحيل وجائز، وإن اختلفت الأفراد والأدلة، وإنما خص المصنف الرُّسلَ، ولم يذكر الأنبياء؛ لأنَّ بعض ما سيأتي ذكره من الواجب لهم خاص بالرسل مثل التبليغ. محي.
(وَمِثْل ذَا) (2) أي: ويجب بالشَّرع أيضاً على كلِّ مكلَّفٍ أَنْ يَعرِفَ مثل ما ذُكر مِن الواجب والجائز والمستحيل (لِرُسُلِهِ) سبحانه، فإنا نعتقد أن الله تعالى كمالات لا نهاية لها غير هذه الصفات الآتي ذكرها، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه نقائص لا نهاية لها، وأنه يجوز في حقه تعالى جائزات لا نهايات لها، فالمراد بالقانون: الدليل الجملي أو المعتقد الإجمالي، وهو المتعين في الجائز؛ إذ لا حد لجزئياته، فيقال كل ممكن يجوز في حقه تعالى فعله وتركه، وكذا نؤمن إجمالا بوجوب الكمالات التي لم يقم دليل على تفصيلها، ولا نهاية لها بحسب عقولنا أو الواقع. وقولهم: كل ما وجد خارجا متناه فهو في الحوادث، والمولى يعلمها تفصيلا ويعلم أنها غير متناه، وتوقف العلم التفصيلي على التناهي باعتبار الحوادث، وبالجملة: فسبحان من لا يعلم قدره غيره ولا يبلغ الواصفون صفته. ويحتمل أن يكون المعنى المراد بتعريف الأقسام الثلاثة معرفة جزئياتها. فإن قلت المقصود من التعريفات معرفة المفهومات. قلنا نعم؛ لكن معرفة المفهومات وسيلة إلى معرفة جزئيات هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، قال إمام الحرمين: إن معرفتها نفس العقل، وإن من لم يعرفها فليس بعاقل، ومراد إمام الحرمين أن العقل معرفة بعض الضروريات من كل منها، ولكن التحقيق أنها ثمرة العقل، لا أنها نفس العقل كما نص عليه بعض المحققين، والله أعلم. الشنواني.
Explanation of the divisions of rational judgment. And included in the mukallaf: the common people, slaves, women, and servants; for they are obligated to know the creedal matters from evidence, when they have the capacity to understand it (1), otherwise imitation (taqlid) suffices them. (1) His statement: 'when they have capacity to understand it' – Imam al-Sanusi refuted that in his 'Sharh al-Wusta' saying: 'Capacity is extremely rare, or non-existent at all, for the apparent is that everyone who has the basic intellect of obligation is capable of knowledge and consideration.' End: i.e., because what is required is the general proof, and it is accessible to everyone who has the basic intellect of obligation. Al-Shanwani. 'Adam' (2) The author indicated by the word 'like' (mithl) that the obligatory in the right of the prophets, peace be upon them, the impossible and the possible are not exactly the same as the obligatory in His right, and the possible and impossible; rather what is meant is likeness in absolute obligatory, impossible, and possible, even if the individuals and proofs differ. And the author specifically mentioned the messengers and did not mention the prophets because some of what will come of the obligatory for them is specific to messengers, such as delivering the message. Muhyi. (And like that) (2) i.e., it is also required by the Law upon every mukallaf to know the like of what was mentioned of the obligatory, possible, and impossible for His messengers, exalted is He. For we believe that Allah has endless perfections other than these attributes to be mentioned later, and that Allah, exalted, it is impossible for Him to have endless deficiencies, and that it is possible in His right, exalted, endless possibilities. So what is meant by the law (al-qanun) is the general proof or the general belief, and it is what is determined in the possible, since there is no limit to its particulars. So it is said: every possible thing it is possible in His right, exalted, to do it or leave it. Likewise we believe in general in the obligation of perfections for which no detailed proof has come, and they are endless according to our intellects or reality. And their statement: 'Everything that exists externally is finite – it is among contingent events (hawadith), and the Lord knows them in detail and knows they are infinite, and detailed knowledge depends on finitude with respect to contingent events.' In summary: Glory be to Him whose measure none knows other than Him, and the describers cannot describe His attribute. And it is possible that what is meant by the definition of the three divisions is knowledge of their particulars. If you say: the purpose of definitions is knowledge of concepts. We say: yes, but knowledge of concepts is a means to knowledge of the particulars of these three divisions. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Knowledge of them is the intellect itself, and whoever does not know them is not intelligent.' And what Imam al-Haramayn meant is that the intellect is knowledge of some necessities from each of them, but the verification is that it is the fruit of the intellect, not that it is the intellect itself, as some verifiers stated. And Allah knows best. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the divisions of rational judgment (aqsam al-hukm al-'aqli) and the obligation to know them. It begins by stating who is included in the mukallaf (obligated person): the common people, slaves, women, and servants. They must know the creedal matters through evidence (adilla), provided they have the capacity (ahliyya) to understand the proofs. If they lack capacity, then imitation (taqlid) suffices. The commentary then includes a rebuttal by al-Sanusi, as reported by al-Shanwani, arguing that capacity is extremely rare or nonexistent; anyone with basic intellect is capable of knowledge and consideration. Next, the author discusses what is required by the Law upon every mukallaf: to know the like (mithl) of the wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for the messengers. The explanation clarifies that the categories are the same, but the specific instances differ. The passage then discusses the concept of the 'law' (qanun) as the general proof or general belief, applicable to the possible because its particulars are limitless. It mentions that we believe in endless perfections for Allah and endless impossibilities. The text also addresses the definition of the three divisions: some say it means knowledge of particulars, but others say definitions are for concepts, not particulars. Imam al-Haramayn's view is cited: knowing the divisions is the intellect itself. However, the verification is that this knowledge is a fruit of the intellect, not the intellect itself. The passage ends with 'Allah knows best.'

مكلف
تقليد
أهلية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only scholars need to know theology; common people can blindly follow without any understanding.
Correction: All mukallaf are obligated to know the basics, but if they lack capacity to understand proofs, imitation suffices. Capacity is generally assumed.

Checkpoint

A) Only scholars
B) Common people, slaves, women, and servants
C) Only men
D) Only prophets

Key Takeaway
All mukallaf, regardless of status, must know the creedal matters, with evidence if able, otherwise imitation.

?

?

The Judgments of Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz for the Prophets
Explanation of the obligation to know the three judgments for the messengers and the meaning of 'like'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the same three categories of rational judgment apply to the prophets, but the specific instances are not identical to those for Allah.

Core Idea
The categories are same, but the details differ between Allah and His prophets.

This lesson focuses on the statement that every mukallaf must know the analogous judgments for the messengers. The author uses the word 'like' to indicate that while the categories are the same, the specific obligations, impossibilities, and possibilities are distinct for prophets.

Guided Path
1
Note the use of 'like' (mithl)
The author emphasizes that the judgments for prophets are like those for Allah but not identical.
This distinction is important to avoid anthropomorphism.
2
Understand the obligation to know
Every mukallaf must know the wajib, mustahil, ja'iz for the messengers.
3
Recognize why messengers are specified
The author focuses on messengers because some obligations are specific to them, like delivering the message.
This shows the unique role of messengers.

بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي
•
ودخل في المكلَّف: العوام، والعبيد، والنِّسوان، والخَدَمُ؛ فإنَّهم مكلَّفونَ بمعرفة العقائد عن الأدلة، متى كان فيهم أهليَّة فهمها (1)، وإلا كفاهم التقليد.
(1) قوله: متى كان فيهم أهلية فهمها ردَّ ذلك الإمام السنوسي في «شرح الوسطى» بقوله: إن الأهلية في غاية الندور، أو ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنظر. اهـ :أي: لأن المطلوب الدليل الجملي، وهو متيسر من كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف. الشنواني.
عدم
(2) أشار المصنف بلفظ: «مثل» إلى أنّ الواجب في حقّ الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، والمستحيل والجائز ليست هي عين الواجب في حقه، والجائز والمستحيل، فالمراد المثلية في مطلق واجب ومستحيل وجائز، وإن اختلفت الأفراد والأدلة، وإنما خص المصنف الرُّسلَ، ولم يذكر الأنبياء؛ لأنَّ بعض ما سيأتي ذكره من الواجب لهم خاص بالرسل مثل التبليغ. محي.
(وَمِثْل ذَا) (2) أي: ويجب بالشَّرع أيضاً على كلِّ مكلَّفٍ أَنْ يَعرِفَ مثل ما ذُكر مِن الواجب والجائز والمستحيل (لِرُسُلِهِ) سبحانه، فإنا نعتقد أن الله تعالى كمالات لا نهاية لها غير هذه الصفات الآتي ذكرها، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه نقائص لا نهاية لها، وأنه يجوز في حقه تعالى جائزات لا نهايات لها، فالمراد بالقانون: الدليل الجملي أو المعتقد الإجمالي، وهو المتعين في الجائز؛ إذ لا حد لجزئياته، فيقال كل ممكن يجوز في حقه تعالى فعله وتركه، وكذا نؤمن إجمالا بوجوب الكمالات التي لم يقم دليل على تفصيلها، ولا نهاية لها بحسب عقولنا أو الواقع. وقولهم: كل ما وجد خارجا متناه فهو في الحوادث، والمولى يعلمها تفصيلا ويعلم أنها غير متناه، وتوقف العلم التفصيلي على التناهي باعتبار الحوادث، وبالجملة: فسبحان من لا يعلم قدره غيره ولا يبلغ الواصفون صفته. ويحتمل أن يكون المعنى المراد بتعريف الأقسام الثلاثة معرفة جزئياتها. فإن قلت المقصود من التعريفات معرفة المفهومات. قلنا نعم؛ لكن معرفة المفهومات وسيلة إلى معرفة جزئيات هذه الأقسام الثلاثة، قال إمام الحرمين: إن معرفتها نفس العقل، وإن من لم يعرفها فليس بعاقل، ومراد إمام الحرمين أن العقل معرفة بعض الضروريات من كل منها، ولكن التحقيق أنها ثمرة العقل، لا أنها نفس العقل كما نص عليه بعض المحققين، والله أعلم. الشنواني.
Explanation of the divisions of rational judgment. And included in the mukallaf: the common people, slaves, women, and servants; for they are obligated to know the creedal matters from evidence, when they have the capacity to understand it (1), otherwise imitation (taqlid) suffices them. (1) His statement: 'when they have capacity to understand it' – Imam al-Sanusi refuted that in his 'Sharh al-Wusta' saying: 'Capacity is extremely rare, or non-existent at all, for the apparent is that everyone who has the basic intellect of obligation is capable of knowledge and consideration.' End: i.e., because what is required is the general proof, and it is accessible to everyone who has the basic intellect of obligation. Al-Shanwani. 'Adam' (2) The author indicated by the word 'like' (mithl) that the obligatory in the right of the prophets, peace be upon them, the impossible and the possible are not exactly the same as the obligatory in His right, and the possible and impossible; rather what is meant is likeness in absolute obligatory, impossible, and possible, even if the individuals and proofs differ. And the author specifically mentioned the messengers and did not mention the prophets because some of what will come of the obligatory for them is specific to messengers, such as delivering the message. Muhyi. (And like that) (2) i.e., it is also required by the Law upon every mukallaf to know the like of what was mentioned of the obligatory, possible, and impossible for His messengers, exalted is He. For we believe that Allah has endless perfections other than these attributes to be mentioned later, and that Allah, exalted, it is impossible for Him to have endless deficiencies, and that it is possible in His right, exalted, endless possibilities. So what is meant by the law (al-qanun) is the general proof or the general belief, and it is what is determined in the possible, since there is no limit to its particulars. So it is said: every possible thing it is possible in His right, exalted, to do it or leave it. Likewise we believe in general in the obligation of perfections for which no detailed proof has come, and they are endless according to our intellects or reality. And their statement: 'Everything that exists externally is finite – it is among contingent events (hawadith), and the Lord knows them in detail and knows they are infinite, and detailed knowledge depends on finitude with respect to contingent events.' In summary: Glory be to Him whose measure none knows other than Him, and the describers cannot describe His attribute. And it is possible that what is meant by the definition of the three divisions is knowledge of their particulars. If you say: the purpose of definitions is knowledge of concepts. We say: yes, but knowledge of concepts is a means to knowledge of the particulars of these three divisions. Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Knowledge of them is the intellect itself, and whoever does not know them is not intelligent.' And what Imam al-Haramayn meant is that the intellect is knowledge of some necessities from each of them, but the verification is that it is the fruit of the intellect, not that it is the intellect itself, as some verifiers stated. And Allah knows best. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the divisions of rational judgment (aqsam al-hukm al-'aqli) and the obligation to know them. It begins by stating who is included in the mukallaf (obligated person): the common people, slaves, women, and servants. They must know the creedal matters through evidence (adilla), provided they have the capacity (ahliyya) to understand the proofs. If they lack capacity, then imitation (taqlid) suffices. The commentary then includes a rebuttal by al-Sanusi, as reported by al-Shanwani, arguing that capacity is extremely rare or nonexistent; anyone with basic intellect is capable of knowledge and consideration. Next, the author discusses what is required by the Law upon every mukallaf: to know the like (mithl) of the wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz for the messengers. The explanation clarifies that the categories are the same, but the specific instances differ. The passage then discusses the concept of the 'law' (qanun) as the general proof or general belief, applicable to the possible because its particulars are limitless. It mentions that we believe in endless perfections for Allah and endless impossibilities. The text also addresses the definition of the three divisions: some say it means knowledge of particulars, but others say definitions are for concepts, not particulars. Imam al-Haramayn's view is cited: knowing the divisions is the intellect itself. However, the verification is that this knowledge is a fruit of the intellect, not the intellect itself. The passage ends with 'Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
رسل
مثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: What is wajib, mustahil, ja'iz for Allah is exactly the same for prophets.
Correction: The categories are the same, but the specific attributes and acts differ.

Checkpoint
Why did the author specifically mention 'messengers' rather than 'prophets'?
A) Because messengers are higher in rank
B) Because some wjib for them is specific to messengers like tabligh
C) Because prophets are not mukallaf
D) There is no reason

Key Takeaway
The rational judgments for prophets are analogous to those for Allah but not identical in detail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of the obligations you have towards Allah, do you ever treat them as just 'like' your obligations towards people, lacking the awe they deserve? How can you remind yourself daily that Allah's commands are categorically different in weight and significance?
?
The lesson shows that even prophets, who are the best of creation, have limited obligations compared to Allah. How does this humble you when you consider your own rank? In what specific area do you need to increase your reverence for Allah's commands, starting today?
?
If the 'possible' for prophets includes human acts like eating and sleeping, what does that teach you about the balance between your human needs and your spiritual duties? Are you neglecting one over the other, and what concrete step can you take to realign your priorities?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

The Law as General Proof and the Intellect's Role in Knowing the Divisions
Explanation of the law and the nature of knowing the three divisions of rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'al-qanun' as the general proof or general belief, and the relationship between knowing the concepts of the three rational divisions and the intellect itself.

Core Idea
The general proof suffices for believing in endless divine perfections and the intellect's knowledge of the three divisions is its fruit, not its essence.

This lesson delves into the meaning of the 'law' (al-qanun) as the general proof that every mukallaf must know. It also explores the nature of defining the three rational judgments and how the intellect relates to this knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'qanun'
The qanun is the general proof (dalil jumli) or general belief (i'tiqad ijmaili). It is used because the particulars of the possible are endless.
2
Grasp the concept of endless perfections
We believe in endless perfections for Allah and that He is free from endless deficiencies.
This is an article of faith, not detailed proof.
3
Analyze the debate on definitions
Some say defining the three divisions means knowing their particulars; but the commentator says definitions are for concepts, which are a means to particulars.
Imam al-Haramayn said knowledge of them is the intellect itself, but the correct view is that it is the fruit of the intellect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must know every single possible action of Allah in detail.
Correction: We believe in general (ijmalan) that all possible things are permissible for Allah, and we affirm endless perfections generally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'law' (al-qanun) in the context of the possible?
A) Detailed proof for each possible
B) The general proof or general belief
C) Imitation (taqlid)
D) The intellect itself

Key Takeaway
The law (qanun) is the general belief that all possible things are permissible for Allah, and knowing the concepts of rational judgments is a means, not an end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the law requires you to believe in Allah's endless perfections even though you cannot list them all. Is your heart truly convinced of Allah's infinite majesty, or do you find yourself doubting when you cannot see the full picture? How will you strengthen your general belief to trust Him beyond what you can comprehend?
?
This lesson corrects the notion that knowledge of the three divisions is the intellect itself. Have you ever taken pride in your theological knowledge, treating it as the measure of your intelligence? Will you now humble yourself and recognize that your intellect is a gift, and its knowledge—however vast—is merely its fruit? What step will you take today to see your learning as a means to draw closer to Allah, not as a source of arrogance?
?
The general proof suffices for the possible because its particulars are infinite. In your daily life, do you demand detailed clarity from Allah before you trust His decree? Are there areas where you insist on knowing 'why' before submitting? Will you embrace the general belief that everything Allah does is possible and good, even when the details elude you? Commit to one situation this week where you will practice that trust without needing a full explanation.

The Twelve Opinions on the First Obligation (Part 1: The List)
Lists opinions 5–12 on the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of opinions held by scholars on the first duty of the accountable person, from knowledge and reflection to doubt and practice.

Core Idea
There are twelve opinions on the first duty; they vary from knowledge to doubt to prayer.

The author begins by presenting the remaining eight opinions (5–12) on what the first obligation of a mukallaf is. These opinions come from various schools and thinkers, each offering a different starting point for one's religious duties. As you read, notice how some opinions focus on inner belief, others on outward acts, and some on the process of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of opinions
Read the Arabic passage from opinion 5 to the end of the list. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: each opinion is introduced with 'wa dhahaba qawmun' or similar.
2
Identify the core of each opinion
For each opinion (5–12), identify what is claimed as the first obligation. Is it a belief, an action, or a state of mind?
Opinion 7 (doubt) has a longer explanation; note how the author clarifies it.
3
Notice the author's critiques
The author sometimes rejects or clarifies opinions. Find examples: which opinions are rejected and why?
Opinions 8 and 9 are rejected because faith and submission require prior knowledge.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Consider why there are so many opinions. What does this diversity tell us about the complexity of determining the very first religious duty?
This sets the stage for the author's analysis in the next lesson.
