ه - وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : التقليد، ثم يجب عليه النظر الموصل إلى المعرفة .
٦ ـ وذهب قوم إلى أن أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : النُّطق بالشهادتين ، ثم يجب عليه البحث عن طريق إثبات مضمون هاتين الشهادتين .
۷ ـ وذهب أبو هاشم وطائفة من المعتزلة إلى أن أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : الشك ومن العلماء من أنكر هذا القول، واستدل على إنكاره بأن الشَّكَ في شيء من العقائد كفر ، فهو مطلوب الزَّوال ، فلا يكون مطلوب الحصول، والذي نراه أنَّ هؤلاء لم يقصدوا بما قالوه أنَّ الشَّلَّ الذي هو إدراك الطَّرف المرجوح مطلوب الحصول كما فهم المعترض عليهم ، وإنَّما أرادوا أن ترديد الفكر بين الإثبات والنفي حتى يصل إلى الجزم هو أول شيء يجب على المكلف، وهذا هو النظر .
۸ - وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أوَّل شيء يجب على المكلَّف هو: الإيمان.
۹ ـ وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أوَّل شيء يجب على المكلف هو: الإسلام. والقولان الأخيرين متقاربان ، أو متحدان إن قلنا: الإيمان والإسلام واحد، ومع هذا فهما مردودان بأنَّ كلَّا من الإيمان والإسلام محتاج إلى المعرفة .
۱۰ ـ وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو: أن يعتقد أنَّ النظر الموصل إلى معرفة الله تعالى واجب عليه ، فإذا اعتقد ذلك نظر ، فإذا نظر وصل إلى المعرفة
۱۱ - وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : فريضة الوقت الذي كلف فيه. ۱۲ - وذهب قوم إلى أنَّ أول شيء يجب على المكلف واحدٌ مِن أمرين: إما المعرفة وإما - التقليد ، فيكونُ مخيَّرًا بين هذين الأمرين، وأيهما فعل أجزأه .
فهذه اثنا عشر قولا للعلماء في بيان أول الواجبات على المكلف، وأنت لو تدبرت في هذه الأمور وجدت بعضها مقاصد اعتقادية كالمعرفة ، وبعضها مقاصد عملية كالصلاة، وبعضها وسائل كالنظر والقصد إليه، وأنت خبير أنَّ كل شيء لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب، ونستطيع بعد هذا أن نقول: إن هذا الخلاف لفظي ; لأنَّ مَن قال: «إن أول الواجبات هو المعرفة ، إنما عنى أنَّ أول الواجبات من المقاصد الاعتقادية هو المعرفة ، ومن قال:=
الله تعالى، أي معرفة وجوب وجوده تعالى، ومعرفة وَحْدته، وصانعيَّتِه للعالم، ومعرفة صفاته وسائر أحكام الألوهية. وأشار بقوله: (وَفِيهِ) أي: وفي تَعيين أوَّلِ الواجبات (خُلْفٌ) أي: اختلاف (مُنتَصِبْ) أي: قائمٌ بين الأئمة سُنِّيِّين كانوا أَوْ لا ، إلَّا أَنَّه لم يقع خلاف بين المسلمين في وجوب معرفة الله تعالى ، ولا في وجوب النَّظر المُوصّل إليها بقَدْر الطاقة البشريَّة ، ولذا جَعَل الخلاف في الأَوَّليَّة إن أول الواجبات هو النظر أو المقدمة الأولى منه ، أو الشَّرُّ على المعنى الذي اخترناه ، أو القصد إلى النظر»، إنما عَنَى وقصد أنَّ ما ذكر أوَّل الواجبات من حيث إنّه يتوقف عليه الواجب الأوّل من المقاصد الاعتقاديَّة، وهلم جرا ، ولعلَّ قول الشارح فيما بعد «لكنه لم يتوصل إليها إلا بالنظر ... الخ يشير إلى طرف من هذا.
Fifth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: taqlid (unquestioning acceptance), then reflection (nazar) leading to knowledge is obligatory upon him. Sixth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: uttering the two testimonies, then he must research the way to prove the content of these two testimonies. Seventh: Abu Hashim and a faction of the Mu'tazila went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: doubt. And some scholars denied this statement, and argued for its denial by [saying] that doubt in any matter of creed is unbelief, so it is required to be removed, so it cannot be required to be obtained. And what we see is that these [people] did not intend by what they said that doubt, which is the perception of the weaker side, is required to be obtained as those objecting to them understood; rather they meant that the oscillation of thought between affirmation and negation until one reaches certainty is the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person, and this is reflection (nazar). Eighth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: faith (iman). Ninth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: submission (islam). And the last two sayings are close, or identical if we say: faith and submission are one, and despite this they are rejected because each of faith and submission is in need of knowledge. Tenth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: that he believe that reflection leading to knowledge of God Most High is obligatory upon him, then when he believes that he reflects, then when he reflects he attains knowledge. Eleventh: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is: the obligation of the time in which he was charged. Twelfth: A group went to that the first thing obligatory upon the accountable person is one of two things: either knowledge or taqlid, so he is given a choice between these two things, and whichever he does suffices him. These are twelve sayings of the scholars regarding the determination of the first obligations upon the accountable person. And if you were to ponder these matters you would find some of them are doctrinal objectives like knowledge, and some are practical objectives like prayer, and some are means like reflection and intending it. And you are aware that everything without which the obligatory is not completed is itself obligatory. And after this we can say: This disagreement is verbal; because he who said: 'The first obligation is knowledge' only meant that the first obligation from among the doctrinal objectives is knowledge, and he who said: 'God Most High, i.e., knowledge of the necessity of His existence Most High, knowledge of His oneness, His being the Creator of the world, knowledge of His attributes and all the rulings of divinity.' And he indicated by his saying: 'And therein (wa fihi) i.e., in the determination of the first of the obligations (khulf) i.e., disagreement (muntasib) i.e., established among the imams, whether Sunni or not, except that no disagreement occurred among the Muslims regarding the obligation of knowledge of God Most High, nor regarding the obligation of reflection leading to it to the extent of human capability. Therefore he made the disagreement about priority: that the first obligation is reflection or the first step of it, or the intention of reflection, or the beginning [of reflection] according to the meaning we have chosen. By this he meant and intended that what is mentioned as the first obligation is so insofar as the first obligation from among the doctrinal objectives depends on it, and so on. And perhaps the statement of the commentator later, 'But one cannot arrive at it except through reflection' ... etc., points to a part of this.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and analysis of the many opinions on what is the very first thing that a person who has reached the age of accountability (mukallaf) is obligated to do. The passage begins by presenting the fifth through twelfth opinions (the first four were on the previous page). The author lists each opinion clearly, sometimes providing a brief critique or clarification. For example, the opinion that the first duty is doubt is explained: the doubt intended is not the state of uncertainty itself, but the mental oscillation before certainty is reached, which is actually a form of reflection (nazar). The author then notes that the opinions about faith (iman) and submission (islam) are rejected because both require prior knowledge. After completing the list, the author reflects that these opinions actually fall into categories: some are doctrinal objectives (like knowledge), some are practical objectives (like prayer), and some are means (like reflection). He then argues that the disagreement is essentially verbal (lafzi) because everyone agrees that the ultimate goal is knowledge of God, and the dispute is only about what comes first in the sequence of obligations. The passage then continues on page 166, explaining that knowledge means knowledge of God's necessary existence, His oneness, His being the Creator, and His attributes. The author comments on the text 'And therein is an established disagreement,' clarifying that there is no disagreement among Muslims that knowledge of God and the reflection leading to it are obligatory; the disagreement is only about which of these is first in priority. He then recasts the various opinions as different ways of expressing the same underlying truth: the first obligation is the means (reflection or its first step) or the goal (knowledge) depending on perspective. The author concludes by saying the disagreement is resolved by understanding that what is labeled 'first obligation' depends on what it is compared to.

According to the author, which two opinions are rejected because they require prior knowledge?
A) Opinion 5 (taqlid) and opinion 6 (uttering shahada)
B) Opinion 8 (iman) and opinion 9 (islam)
C) Opinion 7 (doubt) and opinion 10 (belief in reflection)
D) Opinion 11 (duty of the time) and opinion 12 (choice)

Key Takeaway
The first obligation of a mukallaf has been understood in many ways, from knowledge and reflection to taqlid and practice, but all opinions seek to determine the starting point of religious duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think back to the moment you first considered your faith in God. Was it a matter of imitation, intellectual inquiry, or something else? How does this lesson's spectrum of opinions make you question the depth of your own starting point?
?
The author rejects opinions 8 and 9 because faith and submission require prior knowledge. Is there any aspect of your faith that you hold without knowledge—a practice or belief you inherited but never examined? What would it cost you to subject it to sincere reflection?
?
You have just read twelve opinions on the first duty. If you were to strip away all external influences, what do you honestly believe is the first thing God requires of you right now? Could you defend that answer with reasoning from this lesson, or does it rest on comfort?

The Twelve Opinions on the First Obligation (Part 2: The Verbal Disagreement Resolved)
Analyses the twelve opinions and resolves the disagreement as verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disagreement over the first obligation is considered verbal and how the author categorizes the opinions as means, ends, or practical duties.

Core Idea
The dispute is only about priority; the first obligation in means differs from the first in ends.

After listing the twelve opinions, the author steps back to analyze them. He shows that some opinions target doctrinal goals, others practical actions, and others the means to reach those goals. He then argues that the entire disagreement is merely verbal: everyone agrees that knowledge of God is the ultimate goal, but they differ on which step comes first. This lesson unpacks that analysis and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's categorization
Read the passage from 'And if you were to ponder' onward. Notice how the author groups the opinions into three types: doctrinal objectives, practical objectives, and means.
These categories are: مقاصد اعتقادية, مقاصد عملية, and وسائل.
2
Understand the verbal disagreement argument
The author says the disagreement is lafzi (verbal). Why? Because all agree that knowledge of God is obligatory; the dispute is only about which comes first in the sequence.
The principle: everything without which an obligation is not completed is itself obligatory.
3
Follow the explanation on page 166
The commentator clarifies that knowledge means knowledge of God's existence, oneness, attributes, etc. The disagreement is only about the very first priority among these.
The text 'And therein is an established disagreement' is explained.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this resolution shows unity among scholars. The apparent conflict is resolved by recognizing different perspectives on what 'first' means (first in means vs. first in ends).

٦ ـ وذهب قوم إلى أن أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : النُّطق بالشهادتين ، ثم يجب عليه البحث عن طريق إثبات مضمون هاتين الشهادتين .
۷ ـ وذهب أبو هاشم وطائفة من المعتزلة إلى أن أول شيء يجب على المكلف هو : الشك ومن العلماء من أنكر هذا القول، واستدل على إنكاره بأن الشَّكَ في شيء من العقائد كفر ، فهو مطلوب الزَّوال ، فلا يكون مطلوب الحصول، والذي نراه أنَّ هؤلاء لم يقصدوا بما قالوه أنَّ الشَّلَّ الذي هو إدراك الطَّرف المرجوح مطلوب الحصول كما فهم المعترض عليهم ، وإنَّما أرادوا أن ترديد الفكر بين الإثبات والنفي حتى يصل إلى الجزم هو أول شيء يجب على المكلف، وهذا هو النظر .

The First Obligatory Duty: Knowledge and Speculative Reasoning
Al-Ash'ari's determination of the first wajib and the role of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari considered knowledge of Allah as the first obligation upon every accountable person, and how speculative reasoning becomes obligatory as its means.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is the first duty; reasoning is its necessary path.

Al-Ash'ari begins by establishing the foundational priority: before any act of worship, one must know the One worshipped. This passage sets out his well-known position on the first obligatory duty and shows how speculative reasoning (nazar) becomes compulsory as the only means to attain that knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and find the claim that knowledge is the first obligatory duty upon the accountable person.
Notice the phrase 'المعرفة أول واجب على المكلف'.
2
Understand the proof
Analyze the reason given: all obligations are only realized through knowledge. This makes knowledge a prerequisite for all other duties.
3
Trace the necessity of nazar
See how the author argues that speculative reasoning (nazar) becomes obligatory because knowledge depends on it, and whatever is necessary for an obligation is itself obligatory.
The text says 'فهو واجب بوجوبها'.
4
Reflect on the condition of capacity
Note that nazar is within the capacity of the accountable person, which is a condition for it to be obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعيين أول واجب على المكلف وبيان الخلاف فيه
والمشهور عن الأشعري إمام أهلِ السُّنَّة الذي بُنيت هذه المنظومة على مختارِه أنَّ المعرفة أوَّلُ وَاجب على المكلَّف؛ لأنَّ جميع الواجبات لا تتحقق إلا بها، فَاجْزم اعتقادك به، واختَرْهُ غيرَ مُلتفت إلى غيره لأرجحيَّته، لكنَّه لا يتوصل إليها إلَّا بالنَّظر؛ فهو واجب بوجوبها؛ لتوقفها عليه، مع كونه مقدورًا للمُكلَّف، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو واجب.
Determining the first obligatory duty upon the accountable person and explaining the disagreement about it. The well-known from al-Ash'ari, the Imam of the People of the Sunna upon whose chosen view this poem is built, is that knowledge is the first duty upon the accountable person because all obligations are not realized except through it. So firmly hold your belief in it and choose it, not turning to others, due to its preponderance. But one does not arrive at it except through speculative reasoning; thus it is obligatory due to its obligation because it depends on it, while being within the capacity of the accountable person, and everything that is such is obligatory.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage determines the first obligatory duty (wajib) upon the accountable person (mukallaf). The author states the well-known position of Imam al-Ash'ari that the first duty is knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah. The proof given is that all obligations are not realized (la tatahaqqaq) except through knowledge. Therefore, one must hold this belief firmly and choose it over other opinions. However, knowledge can only be attained through speculative reasoning (nazar), so nazar is also obligatory as a means to the obligatory end. The reasoning is presented in a syllogistic form: nazar is a prerequisite for obligatory knowledge, and whatever is a prerequisite for an obligation is itself obligatory. Additionally, nazar is within the mukallaf's capacity, satisfying the condition for obligation.

Key Terms
أول واجب
المعرفة
النظر
المكلَّف
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that speculative reasoning (nazar) is not obligatory if knowledge is the first duty, believing that one can attain knowledge without reasoning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that nazar is the only means to attain the obligatory knowledge, and since the end is obligatory, the means is also obligatory. Additionally, nazar is within human capacity, so it fulfills all conditions for obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the first obligatory duty according to the well-known position of al-Ash'ari?
A) Speculative reasoning (nazar)
B) Knowledge (ma'rifa)
C) Intention (qasd)
D) Action ('amal)

Key Takeaway
The first duty upon every accountable person is knowledge of Allah, and speculative reasoning is an equally required means to that end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly examined whether your knowledge of Allah is based on thorough reasoning (nazar) or merely on blind imitation? What would it cost you to truly engage your intellect to know your Creator?
?
If knowledge of Allah is the very first duty upon you, how much time and effort have you actually invested in seeking that knowledge compared to other obligations? Does your daily schedule reflect this priority?
?
Knowing that speculative reasoning (nazar) is not optional but obligatory for you, what specific steps will you take this week to exercise your intellect in pondering the signs of Allah in creation and scripture?

Conditions for Valid Nazar
Author states that nazar yields knowledge and enumerates its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary conditions for nazar to yield knowledge, including the distinctions from deniers like the Samaniyya and Isma'iliyya.

Core Idea
Nazar requires life, intent, wakefulness, and absence of prior knowledge or compound ignorance.

The author begins by affirming that nazar (rational reflection) does indeed produce knowledge, contrary to some skeptics. He then lays out the essential conditions that must be met for nazar to be sound and effective. Understanding these conditions helps us differentiate proper theological reasoning from flawed argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Read the first sentence: the author asserts that nazar yields knowledge absolutely, in both divine and other matters. Note the rejection of the Samaniyya and engineers.
2
Note the refutations of opponents
The author refutes the Isma'iliyya by stating nazar does not depend on an infallible teacher. Understand why this is significant.
This establishes independence of rational inquiry.
3
List the conditions for nazar
Identify the six conditions: life, intention, wakefulness, not already knowing, no compound ignorance. Note that the first four are also conditions for knowledge generally.
4
Understand the additional conditions for sound nazar
Sound nazar must be from evidence (dalil) not sophistry (shubha), and must consider the direction of indication (wajh al-dalala). Explain what these mean.
The direction of indication is what moves the mind from evidence to conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنّ النظر يفيد العلم على التحقيق مطلقا في الإلهيات وغيرها، خلافا للسمنية والمهندسين في عدم إفادته إياه، وأنّ النظر يفيد معرفة الله من غير توقف على وجود المعلم يعني المعصوم، خلافا للإسماعيلية، وأن النظر يشترط فيه الحياة والقصد وعدم النوم والغفلة وعدم العلم بالمطلوب؛ إذ لا طلب مع الحصول، وعدم الجهل المركب بالمطلوب؛ لأنه يكون جازما بنقيضه؛ لأن ذلك يمنعه عن الإقدام على الطلب، والأربعةُ الأوَلُ شرطٌ في العلم أيضا، ويزاد لصحيحه أن يكون نظرا في دليل لا في شبهة، وأن يكون النظر فيه من وجه دلالته، وهو ما بواسطته ينتقل الذهن من الدليل إلى المدلول، وأن حصول العلم بعد النظر المكتسب عادي، فلا يختلف عنه إلا خرقا للعادة، كتخلف الإحراق عن النار، وهذا ما ذهب إليه الأشعري، وذهب الرازي إلى أنه عقلي ضروري فلا ينفك عنه أصلا كوجود الجوهر لوجود العرض. فعلى الأول: المشهور أنه يكون مكتسبا للناظر وهو رأي الجمهور؛ لأن حصوله عن نظر المكتسب له. وعلى الثاني: لا يكون كذلك؛ لأن حصوله اضطراري، لا قدرة له على دفعه ولا انفكاك عنه. وبتأمل هذا التقرير تعلم أن الخلاف لفظي، وأن تسميته بالمكتسب أنسب. وكون النظر الصحيح مؤديا إلى العلم إما أن يكون بطريق اللزوم العقلي كالتلازم بين الجوهر والعرض، فوجود أحدهما بدون الآخر مستحيل عقلي لا تتعلق به القدرة، بل إما أن يوجدا معا أو يعدما، وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين الجويني، وهو ما رجحه الأمير والشنواني في حاشيتيهما. وإما أن يكون التلازم عاديا يقبل التخلف، وهو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري. وقالت المعتزلة بالتولد وهو: أن يوجب الفعل لفاعله شيئا آخر. وقالت الفلاسفة بالإيجاب والتعليل.
Know that nazar yields knowledge in actuality absolutely in divine matters and others, contrary to the Samaniyya and the engineers who deny its yielding. And that nazar yields knowledge of God without depending on the existence of a teacher, i.e., the infallible imam, contrary to the Isma'iliyya. And that nazar requires life, intention, absence of sleep and heedlessness, absence of knowledge of the sought, for there is no seeking when it is already attained, and absence of compounded ignorance of the sought, because one would be certain of its opposite, and that prevents embarking on seeking. The first four are also conditions for knowledge. And additionally for sound nazar it must be reasoning from evidence not from a sophism, and the reasoning must be from the aspect of its indication, which is that by which the mind moves from the evidence to the indicated. And the attainment of knowledge after acquired nazar is customary, so it does not differ from it except by a breach of custom, like the failure of fire to burn. This is the view of al-Ash'ari. Al-Razi held that it is rationally necessary and never separable, like the existence of a substance from the existence of an accident. According to the first view, it is well-known that it is acquired for the reasoner, and that is the view of the majority. According to the second, it is not so because its attainment is involuntary, one has no power to repel it or be free from it. Upon reflection, the difference is verbal, and calling it 'acquired' is more appropriate. And the fact that sound nazar leads to knowledge is either by way of rational necessity, like the concomitant relation between substance and accident, so the existence of one without the other is rationally impossible, not subject to power, but they exist together or cease together—this is the view of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, which al-Amir and al-Shanwani preferred in their supercommentaries. Or the concomitance is customary and admits non-occurrence—this is the view of Shaykh al-Ash'ari. The Mu'tazila held the theory of generation (tawallud), that an act necessitates for its agent something else. The philosophers held the theory of necessitation and causation.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on whether nazar necessarily leads to knowledge. It begins by affirming that nazar does yield knowledge in all matters, contradicting the Samaniyya and the engineers. It then distinguishes from the Isma'iliyya by stating that nazar suffices without an infallible teacher. Next, it lists conditions for nazar: life, intention, wakefulness, not already knowing the sought, and not having compound ignorance. Sound nazar must be based on evidence and consider the direction of implication. Then it presents the Ash'ari view that the link is customary and can break by miracle, and the Razi view that it is rationally necessary. The difference is verbal. Finally, it compares three theories: rational necessity (Juwayni), customary concomitance (Ash'ari), and the Mu'tazili theory of generation and the philosophical theory of necessitation.

Key Terms
نظر
شرط
دليل
شبهة
جهل مركب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar automatically produces knowledge without conditions.
Correction: Nazar requires specific conditions like life, intention, and freedom from compound ignorance.

Checkpoint
Which condition is additional for sound nazar beyond the general conditions?
A) Life
B) Intention
C) Reasoning from evidence
D) Absence of sleep

Key Takeaway
Nazar is a disciplined activity with specific prerequisites; without them, it does not yield knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so certain of something false that you refused to seek the truth? What would it take for you to admit you might be wrong about a belief you hold dear?
?
The lesson says nazar requires wakefulness and intent. When you seek knowledge about your Creator, are you truly awake and intentional, or are you just going through motions? How can you change your approach starting today?
?
Do you sometimes outsource your faith to others without actively reasoning from evidence? Are you willing to examine the proof for your beliefs, or do you rely on authority or habit? What step will you take this week to personally verify a core belief?

Views on the Nature of Knowledge from Nazar
Author discusses Ash'ari vs Razi, verbal nature, and three theories of causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different schools' positions on whether the knowledge from nazar is customary, necessary, or generated, and how the dispute is largely verbal.

Core Idea
Ash'ari says knowledge follows nazar by custom; Razi says by rational necessity; but the difference is verbal.

After establishing the conditions for nazar, the author turns to the nature of the knowledge produced. He presents the Ash'ari view that it is customary, the Razi view that it is rationally necessary, and notes that the disagreement is largely terminological. Then he compares three theories on the mechanism: rational concomitance, customary habit, generation, and causation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari view of customary connection
The author says knowledge after nazar is 'adi (customary), like fire burning. It can be broken by miracle (khariq li'l-ada).
2
Contrast with Razi's view of rational necessity
Al-Razi holds it is 'aqli daruri (rationally necessary), inseparable like substance and accident.
3
Note that the dispute is verbal
The author says after reflection the difference is verbal and calling it 'acquired' is more appropriate.
Acquired knowledge (muktasab) vs involuntary (idtirari).
4
Examine the three theories of how nazar leads to knowledge
The author compares rational necessity (Juwayni), custom (Ash'ari), generation (Mu'tazila), and necessitation (philosophers).
Each school has a different theory of causality.

Three Types of Nazar Based on Premises
Classification of nazar according to premise certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how nazar is classified according to the certainty of its premises, and how this determines whether it yields knowledge, belief, or opinion.

Core Idea
The outcome of nazar depends on the certainty of its premises.

This passage from the commentary presents a detailed classification of nazar (speculative reasoning) based on the epistemological status of its premises. Before we can assess whether a nazar is valid, we need to know what kind of conclusion it produces. The author distinguishes three types: those leading to knowledge, belief, and opinion. This classification is foundational for understanding the epistemology of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Begin by reading the first part of the passage, which defines 'nazar' and 'i'tiqad'. Notice how 'i'tiqad is a decisive judgment that can be correct or erroneous.
2
Identify the classification of premises
Focus on the footnote: premises are either certain (مجزوم بها) or conjectural (مظنونة). Then certain premises are subdivided based on the source of certainty: evidence (دليل) or taqlid (تقليد).
3
Match each type to its outcome
See how certain-by-evidence leads to knowledge (علم), certain-by-taqlid leads to belief (اعتقاد), and conjectural premises lead to opinion (ظن). The examples clarify each case.
4
Contrast knowledge and belief
The passage emphasizes that both knowledge and belief are decisive judgments, but only knowledge is based on evidence. This distinction is crucial in kalam.
See the misconception about conflating belief with knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله أو ظنّ (۱) ، والاعتقاد هو الحكمُ الجازم القابل للتغير، ويكون صحيحا إِنْ طابق الواقع ، كاعتقاد المقلد سُنيَّة الضُّحى، وفاسدا إن لم يطابقه ، كاعتقاد الفلسفي قدم العالم.
ووجوبُ النَّظرِ عندنا بالشَّرع كالمعرفة ، وقد تقدَّمَ التّصريح به معها، فلذا تركه هنا .
(۱) اعلم أنَّ مقدمات النَّظر ، أي : الدَّليل إما أن تكون مجزوما بها ، وإما أن تكون مظنونةٌ . والأوَّل: إما أن يكون سبب الجزم هو الاستدلال عليها ، وإما أن يكون سبب الجزم هو التقليد. وإِنَّما يكون الفكر موصلا إلى علم ، إذا كانت مقدماته مجزوما بها عن دليل، كقولك: «العالم متغيّر وكل متغيّر حادث ، إذا قلت هذا وأنت تعرف أنَّ دليلَ الصُّغرى : المشاهدة على ما قلنا قبل ، ودليل الكبرى : أنه يستحيل أن يطرأ العدمُ على القديم، ويكون الفكر موصلا إلى اعتقاد إن كانت مقدماته مجزوما بها عن تقليد لا عن نظر ، كقولك: «العالم حادث وكلُّ حادث فلا بدَّ له مِن محدث»، إذا قلت ذلك مقلدا ، ولكنَّك جازم بكلِّ مقدِّمة من المقدمتين ، وإِنَّما يكون الفكر موصلا إلى الظَّنّ إذا كانت مقدماته أو بعضها مظنونا بها كقولك: «هذا رجل يدور بالسلاح في الليل، فهو لص». محي.
Nazar regarding the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God or opinion (1). And 'i'tiqad (belief) is the decisive judgment that is subject to change; it is correct if it corresponds to reality, like the belief of the muqallid (blind follower) in the supererogatory prayer of forenoon, and corrupt if it does not correspond, like the belief of the philosopher in the pre-eternity of the world. And the obligation of nazar with us is by revelation, like knowledge, and the explicit statement of it has already preceded with it, so he left it here. (1) Know that the premises of nazar, i.e., the evidence, are either certain or conjectural. The first is either such that the cause of certainty is inference to them, or such that the cause is taqlid. Thought only leads to knowledge when its premises are certain by evidence, like your saying: 'The world is changing and everything changing is originated'—when you say this knowing that the proof of the minor premise is observation as we said before, and the proof of the major premise is that it is impossible for non-existence to occur to the eternal. And thought leads to belief when its premises are certain by taqlid not by nazar, like your saying: 'The world is originated and everything originated must have an originator'—when you say that as a muqallid, but you are certain of each of the two premises. And thought only leads to opinion when its premises or some of them are conjectural, like your saying: 'This man carries a weapon at night, so he is a thief.' Muhyi.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage first restates the definition of nazar as consideration of the self and the world leading to knowledge of God or opinion. It then defines 'i'tiqad (belief) as a decisive judgment that can be correct or corrupt depending on correspondence with reality. The commentator then briefly notes that the obligation of nazar is by revelation (shar') not reason, but he does not elaborate because it was already mentioned. The main content is a long footnote explaining the types of nazar based on the premises. The premises (مقدمات) are divided into certain (مجزوم بها) and conjectural (مظنونة). Certain premises are further divided: if the certainty comes from inference (دليل), the resulting nazar leads to knowledge (علم); if from taqlid (blind acceptance), it leads to belief (اعتقاد). Conjectural premises lead to opinion (ظن). Examples are given: the syllogism 'world is changing, every changing thing is originated' with known proofs leads to knowledge; the same syllogism accepted on authority leads to belief; and an argument from circumstantial evidence (man with weapon at night) leads to opinion. The commentator (Muhyi) organizes these classifications clearly.

Key Terms
مقدمات
مجزوما بها
مظنونة
دليل
تقليد
علم
اعتقاد
ظن
وجوب
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any decisive belief counts as knowledge.
Correction: Knowledge (علم) requires that the premises be certain and that certainty comes from evidence, not from blind acceptance (taqlid).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of premises lead to belief (i'tiqad)?
A) Premises that are certain by evidence
B) Premises that are certain by taqlid
C) Premises that are conjectural
D) Premises that are both certain and conjectural

Key Takeaway
The outcome of nazar depends on whether its premises are certain by evidence (knowledge), certain by taqlid (belief), or conjectural (opinion).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that the world is created, is your certainty based on your own reasoning from evidence, or on what you have been taught (taqlid)? How does this affect the strength of your conviction in the face of doubts?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you hold strong beliefs but have never personally examined the proofs? Will you commit to studying at least one proof from the Quran or nature this week to transform that belief into knowledge?
?
What beliefs do you hold that are based on conjecture rather than certainty? How does that influence your actions and reliance on God? Would you be willing to seek greater certainty in one of those areas starting today?

The Proof from the Soul's Contingent Attributes
Al-Sharqawi lists the soul's changing attributes to prove its need for a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the changing attributes of the soul prove its contingency and the existence of a necessary Creator.

Core Idea
Your soul's changing attributes are evidence that it is created, not eternal.

Al-Sharqawi presents a compelling argument rooted in self-observation: the soul is a locus of countless attributes that are constantly changing. This change indicates that these attributes are originated, and since they inhere in the soul, the soul itself must also be originated. Let's read the passage and unpack this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the extensive list of attributes and the phrase 'All of them are changing.'
The author uses a long list to show comprehensiveness.
2
Identify the categories of attributes
The passage lists sensory, quantitative, emotional, visual, mental, and religious attributes. Observe that they all change: they come into existence and cease to exist.
Change is the key sign of origination (ḥudūth).
3
Understand the logical inference
The author concludes that changing attributes are originated and require a Maker. Since these attributes inhere in the soul and are inseparable from it, the soul itself must be originated as well.
This is the core of the proof: the soul is contingent because its attributes are contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفس والعالم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله على وجوب وجود صانعك وصفاته، فإنها مشتملة على سمع، وبصر، وكلام، وطولٍ وعَرض، وعُمق، ورضا، وغضب، وبياض، وحمرة، وسَوادٍ ، وعِلمٍ ، وجهل ، وإيمان ، وكُفر ، ولذَّةٍ، وألم ، وغير ذلك مما لا يُحصى، وكلُّها متغيّرة وخارجةٌ مِن العدم إلى الوجود، ومن الوجود إلى العدم، وذلك دليل الحدوثِ والافتقار إلى صانع حكيم واجب الوجود ، عام العلم ، تامّ القدرة والإرادة، فتكون حادثةً، قائمة بالذات لازمة لها، وهي وملازم الحادثِ حادث أيضا.
The consideration of the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God upon the obligation of the existence of your Maker and His attributes. For it (the soul) includes hearing, sight, speech, length, breadth, depth, pleasure, anger, whiteness, redness, blackness, knowledge, ignorance, faith, disbelief, pleasure, pain, and other countless things. All of them are changing and emerging from non-existence into existence, and from existence into non-existence, and that is evidence of origination (al-ḥudūth) and need for a Wise Maker, Necessary of Existence, universal in knowledge, perfect in power and will. So they are originated, subsisting in the essence, necessary for it, and it (the essence) and the concomitant of the originated are also originated.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the soul's own attributes for the existence of a necessary Creator. First, it notes that the soul contains a wide range of attributes: sensory (hearing, sight, speech), quantitative (length, breadth, depth), emotional (pleasure, anger), visual (whiteness, redness, blackness), mental (knowledge, ignorance), religious (faith, disbelief), and sensory experiences (pleasure, pain). All of these are observed to change, coming from non-existence to existence and back. This change (taghayyur) is a sign of origination (ḥudūth), which means they need a Maker who is Necessary, Wise, All-Knowing, and Omnipotent. Hence these attributes are originated. Since they subsist in the soul and are necessary for it, the soul itself must be originated, because whatever is the locus of originated accidents and is inseparable from them is itself originated. This final inference is summarized: 'and it and the concomitant of the originated are also originated,' meaning the soul (the knower) and its inseparable relation to these originated attributes is itself originated.

الصانع
الحدوث
العرض
الملازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is eternal and has no beginning.
Correction: The passage shows that the soul's attributes are constantly changing, which proves they are originated. Since these attributes are inseparable from the soul, the soul itself must be originated and not eternal.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author provide to prove the soul's contingency?
A) The soul's attributes are constant.
B) The soul's attributes change.
C) The soul is self-existent.
D) The soul is not created.

Key Takeaway
The soul's changing attributes demonstrate its origination and dependency on a necessary Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your own soul—your knowledge, your moods, your beliefs. Can you honestly name a single attribute that has never changed? If all are changing, what does that force you to admit about your own existence and your need for a Creator?
?
The passage says the soul's attributes are 'necessary for it'—you cannot be a soul without them. If every one of these attributes is originated, what does that mean for your claim to be self-sufficient? Are you truly independent of God?
?
Consider your faith (iman) and disbelief (kufr) as attributes mentioned in the passage. If even your faith is a changing attribute that comes and goes, what does that imply about your need for a constant Creator? How does this affect your reliance on Him moment by moment?

Proof of the World's Contingency from Its Accidents
Explanation of the world's contingency proof via accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence of temporal accidents in the world demonstrates its contingency and leads to the proof that it has a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's changing accidents prove it is not eternal but created.

Even though the world displays a marvelously perfect design, that very perfection points to a wise Creator—but it does not prove the world is eternal. The key to proving its temporality (حدوث) lies in observing the inseparable accidental changes like motion and rest. This lesson unpacks the logical syllogism that uses these accidents to argue that the world must have a beginning and therefore a Maker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the poem lines are embedded within the commentary.
The passage combines two lines of poetry with explanatory prose.
2
Identify the two key elements
Find the reference to 'creation of wonderful wisdom' (صنع بديع الحكم) and the 'proof of non-existence' (دليل العدم). Understand their roles: perfection indicates the Creator, but accidents indicate temporality.
The commentator emphasizes that perfection alone might suggest eternity, but the exception 'but' (لكن) introduces the real proof.
3
Understand the syllogism
Trace the minor premise ('the world is subject to non-existence') and the major premise ('everything subject to non-existence cannot be eternal'). See how the conclusion follows.
The minor premise is derived from observing that all worldly things are either substances or accidents, and accidents accept non-existence.
4
Reflect on the extended argument
Notice the optional extension: the world is temporal, therefore needs an agent. This connects contingency to the existence of a Creator.
This is a common pattern in kalam: proving temporality then deducing a Maker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَجِدْ بِهِ صُنْعَا بَدِيعَ الحِكَمِ لَكِنْ بِهِ قَامَ دَلِيلُ العَدَمِ فإِنَّكَ إِنْ تَنْظُرُ في أحوال ما ذُكِر تَجِدْ بِهِ أي : تَعلَمُ وتتحقَّقُ فيما ذُكِرَ صُنْعًا بَدِيعَ الحِكَمِ أي: الإتقانِ الدَّالَّ على علم صانعه، وقدرته ، وإرادته، و حياته ، واختياره ؛ لأنَّ الإتقان لا يصدرُ إِلَّا عَمَّنْ اتّصفَ بما ذُكر . وما يُشعر به قوله: «بديع الحكم من قدمه، حيث كان كذلك .. يدفعه الاستدراك بقوله: (لَكِنْ) العالَمُ وإن كانَ على غاية من الإتقان هو حادث ؛ لأَنَّه (به) لا بغيره قَامَ دَلِيلُ العَدَم وهي الأعراض الحادثة المُلازمة له، كالحركة والسُّكون التي لا تقوم بغير الحادثِ ؛ فإذا أردتَ أنْ تأتي بقياس مستنبط من نظركَ في العالم لتتوصل به إلى تحقق حدوثه ، قلت: العالم مِن عرشه لفَرشِه جائز عليه العدم وهذه المقدّمة الصُّغرى المطويَّةُ لفهمها من الاستدراك، وبيان هذه المقدمة : أنا اختبرنا الموجود من العالم فوجدناه غير خارج عن الأعيان والأعراض، وهي حادثة لقبولها للعدم، ولو كانت قديمة .. ما طرأ العدم عليها ، والمقدمة الكبرى هي قوله: ١٧ - وكُلُّ ما جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعًا يَسْتَحِيْلُ القِدَمُ ١٧ - وَكُلُّ مَا جَازَ عَلَيْهِ العَدَمُ يعني الفَنَاءُ عَلَيْهِ قَطْعًا يَسْتَحِيلُ أي: يمتنع القدم فيُنتج ذلك: أنَّ العالَمَ حادِثٌ . وإن شئتَ قلت: «العَالَم مفتقر إلى مؤثر ، لأنَّه مُحدَثٌ ، وكلُّ مُحْدَثٍ فله مؤثّر فيُنتج القياسُ : أنَّ العالم له مؤثر .
You will find in it a creation of wonderful wisdom, but through it the proof of non-existence is established. For if you look at the states of what was mentioned, you will find in it, i.e., you will know and verify in what was mentioned, a creation of wonderful wisdom, i.e., perfection that indicates the knowledge of its Maker, His power, His will, His life, and His choice; because perfection does not come except from one who is described with what was mentioned. And what is suggested by his saying 'wonderful wisdom from His eternity, since it was thus,' is rebutted by the exception with his saying 'but': the world, even if it is at the utmost perfection, is temporal; because through it, not through another, the proof of non-existence is established, which are the temporal accidents inseparable from it, like motion and rest, which do not subsist in other than the temporal. So if you wish to produce a syllogism derived from your contemplation of the world to arrive at verifying its temporality, you say: the world, from its throne to its carpet, is subject to non-existence. This is the minor premise, implied by the exception, and the explanation of this premise: we tested the existent of the world and found it not outside of substances and accidents, and they are temporal because they accept non-existence; if they were eternal, non-existence would not occur to them. And the major premise is his saying: '17 - And everything that is subject to non-existence, eternity is necessarily impossible for it.' 17 - And everything that is subject to non-existence, i.e., annihilation, eternity is necessarily impossible for it, i.e., prevented. So that produces the result: that the world is temporal. And if you wish, you say: the world is in need of an agent, because it is temporal, and every temporal has an agent. So the syllogism produces: that the world has an agent.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage explains the proof of the world's contingency (حدوث) from its observable accidents. It begins by quoting the poem: 'You will find in it a creation of wonderful wisdom, but through it the proof of non-existence is established.' The commentary then clarifies: when you contemplate the states of the world, you find a marvelously perfect creation, which points to the Creator's knowledge, power, will, life, and choice—since such perfection can only come from a being possessing these attributes. However, the phrase 'but through it' (لكن به) introduces an exception: the world is contingent precisely because it is associated with temporal accidents that are inseparable from it—like motion and rest—which are themselves contingent and can only subsist in a temporal entity. The argument then constructs a syllogism: (Minor Premise) 'The world, from its throne to its carpet, is subject to non-existence'—this is derived from observing that all worldly things are either substances or accidents, and accidents are temporal because they accept non-existence and change. (Major Premise) 'Everything subject to non-existence is necessarily impossible for it to be eternal.' This is the second poem line. The conclusion is that the world is temporal (حادث). Optionally, one can extend the syllogism: 'The world is in need of an agent (مؤثر) because it is temporal, and everything temporal has an agent, therefore the world has an agent.'

دليل العدم
الأعراض الحادثة
جواز العدم
قدم
حدوث
مؤثر
قياس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The perfect order and wisdom in the world prove that the world itself is eternal.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while perfection indicates a wise Creator, it does not make the world eternal. On the contrary, the world's inseparable changing accidents (like motion and rest) prove it is temporal and contingent.

Checkpoint
What is the 'proof of non-existence' (دليل العدم) that establishes the world's contingency?
A) The world's perfect design.
B) The presence of temporal accidents like motion and rest.
C) The fact that the world is created.
D) The need for an agent.

Key Takeaway
The world's inseparability from temporal accidents proves it is not eternal, and thus requires a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see the perfect order of the world every day—your own body, the sunrise, the seasons. Yet the passage tells you that this very perfection hides a vulnerability: it is inseparable from change and thus cannot be eternal. When you look at something beautiful and well-ordered in your life, do you assume it will last forever? How does knowing that everything except God is subject to non-existence change how you hold onto it? What would it mean for you to live today with the certainty that every blessing you have could be taken away?
?
The passage argues that the world's accidents—like motion and rest—prove it is not eternal. Think of your own constant changes: your moods, your health, your age. Do you live as if you are permanent, or do you daily acknowledge that you are contingent from moment to moment? What would change in your prayer, your attachments, your fears if you fully internalized that you are always in need of the Agent who sustains you?
?
The proof ends with the world needing an agent because it is temporal. But the passage also says that the world's perfection points to the Creator's attributes. You might intellectually agree that God is the Maker, but have you let the fact of your own temporality drive you to actually rely on Him as your Sustainer right now? What one thing today could you do to remind yourself that you are not self-sufficient, but are being held in existence by the One who never changes?

The First Proof: Iman is an Act of the Heart
Sanusi's first proof for iman as tasdiq from nusus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic and hadith texts establish that iman is solely tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), and why the linguistic interpretation is insufficient.

Core Idea
The heart’s act is only tasdiq, so iman must be tasdiq.

Sanusi opens his first proof by citing several Quranic verses and a prophetic supplication that consistently locate iman in the heart. He then argues that since the heart’s only relevant act is tasdiq, iman must be identical with tasdiq. He also addresses a potential objection based on the linguistic meaning of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from textual evidence to logical conclusion.
The passage includes an objection and its refutation.
2
Identify the textual evidence
Find the Quranic verses and the hadith that place iman in the heart. Understand how each supports the point.
The verses are from Surah al-Mujadila, al-Hujurat, and an-Nahl.
3
Follow the argument to tasdiq
Sanusi concludes that since iman is an act of the heart, and the heart’s act is only tasdiq, iman must be tasdiq. Trace this reasoning.
4
Examine the refutation
The objection claims the texts refer to linguistic iman. Sanusi responds that Shar'i terms are transferred to new meanings. See how he argues this.
This shows why linguistic definitions cannot override Shar'i usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويؤيد هذا المذهب وجوه: أحدها: - وقد أشار إليه الشارح - أنَّ استعمال القرآن الكريم في عدة آيات واستعمال الحديث أيضا ، جرياً على أنَّ محل الإيمان هو القلب، قال الله تعالى: ﴿أُوْلَبِكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَنَ ﴾ [المجادلة: ٢٢] ، وقال سبحانه: ﴿وَلَمَّا يَدْخُلِ الْإِيمَنُ فِي قُلُوبِكُمْ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٤] ، وقال جل ذكره: ﴿إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَنُ بِالْإِيمَنِ ﴾ [النحل: ١٠٦]، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك ، فدلت هذه النصوص ونظائرها على أنّ الإيمان فعل القلب ، وليس فعل القلب إلَّا التصديق ، ولا يجوز لقائل أن يقول: إنَّ المراد في هذه النصوص بالإيمان هو الإيمان اللُّغوي، ويُسلَّم أنَّ الإيمان اللغوي هو التصديق وحده ومحله القلب، ينافي أنَّ الإيمان الشرعي يشتمل على الإقرار أو غيره على أنَّه جزء من حقيقته، لأنَّا نقول: إن الإيمان من نقلت الألفاظ التي في عرف الشرع إلى معنى مخصوص ؛ فيجب أن يحمل لفظه على هذا المعنى في خطاب الشرع. الوجه الثَّاني: وأشار إليه الشارح أيضا أنه سبحانه جعل الإيمان شرطًا لصحة الأعمال في نحو قوله جل ذكره: ﴿وَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِنَ الصَّلِحَاتِ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ [طه: ۱۱۲]، ونحن نعلم أن الشرط غير المشروط ، وهذا يصلح للرَّدِّ على من جعل الإيمان هو الطاعات وحدها أو مع التصديق والإقرار . الوجه الثالث: أنه أثبت الإيمان لمن ترك بعض الأعمال في نحو قوله سبحانه:=
And this madhhab is supported by aspects: The first: - and the commentator has alluded to it - that the usage of the Noble Quran in several verses and the usage of the hadith as well, proceeding on the basis that the locus of iman is the heart. Allah Most High said: "Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed iman" (Quran 58:22), and He said: "and when iman has not yet entered into your hearts" (Quran 49:14), and He said: "except one who is forced while his heart is at rest with iman" (Quran 16:106), and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion." So these texts and their likes indicate that iman is an act of the heart, and the act of the heart is nothing but tasdiq. And it is not permissible for anyone to say that what is meant by iman in these texts is the linguistic iman, and it is admitted that linguistic iman is tasdiq alone and its locus is the heart, which contradicts that the shar'i iman includes iqrar or other things as part of its reality, because we say: iman is among the words that in the terminology of the Sharia have been transferred to a specific meaning; so its word must be interpreted according to this meaning in the discourse of the Sharia. The second aspect: - and the commentator has alluded to it as well - that He (glory be to Him) made iman a condition for the validity of deeds in something like His saying: "And whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer" (Quran 20:112), and we know that the condition is other than the conditioned, and this serves as a refutation of those who make iman to be acts of obedience alone or with tasdiq and iqrar. The third aspect: that He affirmed iman for one who abandoned some deeds in something like His saying:
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) supporting the view that iman is only tasdiq. The first proof: Quran and hadith consistently place iman in the heart, e.g., 'He wrote iman in their hearts,' 'iman has not entered your hearts,' 'his heart at rest with iman,' and the Prophet's supplication. Since the heart's act is only tasdiq, iman must be tasdiq. If someone claims these refer to linguistic iman, that fails because the Sharia transfers words to new meanings; thus the shar'i iman must be understood in its special sense, which includes only tasdiq. The second proof: Allah makes iman a condition for the validity of deeds (e.g., 'whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer'). The condition is different from the conditioned; thus iman cannot be the deeds themselves or include them. This refutes views that include actions in the definition. The third proof (incomplete): Allah affirms iman for those who have omitted some deeds, as in a verse not fully cited (likely referring to believers who commit sins but are still called believers). The author shows that iman persists even when actions are lacking, so actions are not part of iman's reality.

Key Terms
الوجوه
فعل القلب
التصديق
الإيمان اللغوي
الإيمان الشرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Quranic verses refer to the linguistic meaning of iman, which could include actions.
Correction: Sanusi argues that Shar'i usage transfers the word to a specific meaning; even if linguistic iman is tasdiq, the Shar'i iman is tasdiq alone, not including actions.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's first proof, what is the only act of the heart that can be iman?
A) Iqrar (verbal confession)
B) Tasdiq (affirmation)
C) Actions of the limbs
D) Both tasdiq and iqrar

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's first proof shows that because the heart's only act is tasdiq, iman must be solely tasdiq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman is purely the heart's affirmation, then your lips have said 'I believe' many times, but has your heart ever truly uttered that affirmation? When was the last time you felt a genuine, wholehearted tasdiq that moved you to tears or transformed your actions—not because of habit, but because the truth seized your soul? What is blocking that today?
?
Sanusi insists that the act of the heart is only tasdiq. But if your heart affirmed the truth of everything in the Quran, wouldn't your life look utterly different? Where is the gap between what you claim to believe and how you actually live? Commit to one specific area this week where you will ask Allah to make your heart's tasdiq so real that it changes your conduct.
?
You know that the Quran says 'Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed iman.' Does your heart feel inscribed, or does faith seem like a surface-level label? What would it take for you to beg Allah tonight to truly inscribe iman into your heart, not just as a memory, but as a burning certainty that alters your fears, desires, and priorities?

The Second Proof: Iman as Condition for Actions
Sanusi's second proof from conditionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conditionality of iman for deeds demonstrates that iman is distinct from and not part of actions.

Core Idea
The condition is different from the conditioned.

Sanusi's second proof uses the logical relationship of condition and conditioned. Since Allah makes iman a condition for the validity of deeds, they must be distinct entities. This refutes any definition that includes actions as part of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. It is concise.
The proof is based on a Quranic verse and basic logic.
2
Understand the conditional relationship
Sanusi cites the verse that conditions righteous deeds on iman. Then he invokes the principle that the condition is not the conditioned.
This is a straightforward logical argument.
3
See the refutation
The proof directly refutes those who include actions (or even iqrar) in the definition of iman.

ويؤيد هذا المذهب وجوه: أحدها: - وقد أشار إليه الشارح - أنَّ استعمال القرآن الكريم في عدة آيات واستعمال الحديث أيضا ، جرياً على أنَّ محل الإيمان هو القلب، قال الله تعالى: ﴿أُوْلَبِكَ كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَنَ ﴾ [المجادلة: ٢٢] ، وقال سبحانه: ﴿وَلَمَّا يَدْخُلِ الْإِيمَنُ فِي قُلُوبِكُمْ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٤] ، وقال جل ذكره: ﴿إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَنُ بِالْإِيمَنِ ﴾ [النحل: ١٠٦]، وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: (اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك ، فدلت هذه النصوص ونظائرها على أنّ الإيمان فعل القلب ، وليس فعل القلب إلَّا التصديق ، ولا يجوز لقائل أن يقول: إنَّ المراد في هذه النصوص بالإيمان هو الإيمان اللُّغوي، ويُسلَّم أنَّ الإيمان اللغوي هو التصديق وحده ومحله القلب، ينافي أنَّ الإيمان الشرعي يشتمل على الإقرار أو غيره على أنَّه جزء من حقيقته، لأنَّا نقول: إن الإيمان من نقلت الألفاظ التي في عرف الشرع إلى معنى مخصوص ؛ فيجب أن يحمل لفظه على هذا المعنى في خطاب الشرع. الوجه الثَّاني: وأشار إليه الشارح أيضا أنه سبحانه جعل الإيمان شرطًا لصحة الأعمال في نحو قوله جل ذكره: ﴿وَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِنَ الصَّلِحَاتِ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ [طه: ۱۱۲]، ونحن نعلم أن الشرط غير المشروط ، وهذا يصلح للرَّدِّ على من جعل الإيمان هو الطاعات وحدها أو مع التصديق والإقرار . الوجه الثالث: أنه أثبت الإيمان لمن ترك بعض الأعمال في نحو قوله سبحانه:=
And this madhhab is supported by aspects: The first: - and the commentator has alluded to it - that the usage of the Noble Quran in several verses and the usage of the hadith as well, proceeding on the basis that the locus of iman is the heart. Allah Most High said: "Those are the ones in whose hearts He has inscribed iman" (Quran 58:22), and He said: "and when iman has not yet entered into your hearts" (Quran 49:14), and He said: "except one who is forced while his heart is at rest with iman" (Quran 16:106), and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion." So these texts and their likes indicate that iman is an act of the heart, and the act of the heart is nothing but tasdiq. And it is not permissible for anyone to say that what is meant by iman in these texts is the linguistic iman, and it is admitted that linguistic iman is tasdiq alone and its locus is the heart, which contradicts that the shar'i iman includes iqrar or other things as part of its reality, because we say: iman is among the words that in the terminology of the Sharia have been transferred to a specific meaning; so its word must be interpreted according to this meaning in the discourse of the Sharia. The second aspect: - and the commentator has alluded to it as well - that He (glory be to Him) made iman a condition for the validity of deeds in something like His saying: "And whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer" (Quran 20:112), and we know that the condition is other than the conditioned, and this serves as a refutation of those who make iman to be acts of obedience alone or with tasdiq and iqrar. The third aspect: that He affirmed iman for one who abandoned some deeds in something like His saying:
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) supporting the view that iman is only tasdiq. The first proof: Quran and hadith consistently place iman in the heart, e.g., 'He wrote iman in their hearts,' 'iman has not entered your hearts,' 'his heart at rest with iman,' and the Prophet's supplication. Since the heart's act is only tasdiq, iman must be tasdiq. If someone claims these refer to linguistic iman, that fails because the Sharia transfers words to new meanings; thus the shar'i iman must be understood in its special sense, which includes only tasdiq. The second proof: Allah makes iman a condition for the validity of deeds (e.g., 'whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer'). The condition is different from the conditioned; thus iman cannot be the deeds themselves or include them. This refutes views that include actions in the definition. The third proof (incomplete): Allah affirms iman for those who have omitted some deeds, as in a verse not fully cited (likely referring to believers who commit sins but are still called believers). The author shows that iman persists even when actions are lacking, so actions are not part of iman's reality.

المشروط
الطاعات
الإقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that iman includes actions, based on verses that link them.
Correction: Sanusi shows that linking as condition-conditioned implies separation, not inclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why can't iman be the acts of obedience themselves?
A) Because the verse mentions both iman and deeds
B) Because iman is a condition for deeds, and the condition is not the conditioned
C) Because the Prophet said so
D) Because deeds are not mentioned in the heart

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that iman and actions are distinct because iman is a condition for actions, not part of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform good deeds, do you ever secretly believe your actions are the proof of your faith? How does this lesson force you to separate your deeds from your iman?
?
If iman is the condition and deeds are the conditioned, what does that say about times you may have done good deeds while your iman was weak? How does this truth change the way you evaluate your own spiritual state?
?
Are you willing today to examine your heart and ask: Am I prioritizing the condition (iman) or the conditioned (deeds)? What one concrete step can you take to strengthen your iman before your next act of worship?

Four Proofs That Iman Is Tasdiq Alone
Al-Laqqani presents four proofs for the doctrine that iman is tasdiq alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five proofs (first, second, third, fifth) that Al-Laqqani presents to support the view that iman (faith) is solely tasdiq (assent of the heart).

Core Idea
Four distinct arguments from Quran and reason prove that iman is only tasdiq.

Al-Laqqani now marshals textual and logical evidence for the position that faith is nothing but inner conviction. Each proof challenges alternative definitions that include outward actions or verbal confession. As you read, note how each argument uses a different method: scriptural, conditional, contextual, and grammatical.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof: iman is an act of the heart
Notice how Quran and hadith locate faith in the heart, showing it is an inner act of tasdiq. Also observe the rebuttal against the 'linguistic faith' objection.
The author insists that legal iman has a transferred meaning.
2
Study the second proof: condition vs. conditioned
Allah makes iman a condition for the validity of works; condition and conditioned are distinct, so iman cannot include works.
This refutes those who define iman as acts alone or acts plus attestation.
3
Examine the third proof: believers who fight
The Quran calls fighting parties 'believers' despite their sin; if works were part of iman, sin would negate it, but it doesn't.
This shows that iman remains even when some obligations are neglected.
4
Analyze the fifth proof: conjunction of works to iman
In verses like 'believed and done righteous works', the conjunction indicates that works are separate from iman, not part of it.
Grammatically, one does not conjoin a part to the whole or equivalent to equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And this school is supported by proofs: First: - and the commentator (al-sharih) pointed to it - that the usage of the Noble Quran in several verses and the usage of the hadith as well, according to the principle that the locus of faith is the heart. Allah Most High said: 'Those are they in whose hearts He has inscribed faith' [58:22], and He said: 'And when faith has not yet entered your hearts' [49:14], and He said: 'Except him who is forced while his heart is at rest with faith' [16:106], and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' These texts and their like indicate that faith is an act of the heart, and the act of the heart is nothing but attestation (tasdiq). It is not permissible for anyone to say that what is meant by faith in these texts is linguistic faith (iman lughawi) and then to concede that linguistic faith is attestation alone and its locus is the heart, which contradicts the fact that legal faith (iman shar'i) includes confession (iqrar) or other things as part of its reality. For we say: Faith is among the words that in the custom of the Law (shar') have been transferred to a specific meaning; therefore its word must be understood in that meaning in the discourse of the Law. Second proof: - and the commentator also pointed to it - that He (glorified) made faith a condition for the validity of good works in such as His saying: 'And whoever does righteous deeds while he is a believer' [20:112]. And we certainly know that the condition is different from the conditioned. This suffices as a refutation of those who make faith the acts of obedience alone or along with attestation and confession. Third proof: that He affirmed faith for those who leave some good works in such as His saying: 'And if two parties of the believers fight' [49:9] the verse. And if good works were part of the reality of faith, the reality would be negated by the negation of a part of it. This is also taken from the words of the commentator. Fifth proof: [Thus in the margin, omitting the fourth proof, and perhaps its numbering is a mistake] that He (glorified) joined good works to faith by conjunction ('atf) in many verses, among them His saying: 'Indeed those who have believed and done righteous deeds, for them are the gardens of Firdaws as a lodging' [18:107]. There is no doubt that the original principle is that the conjoined item (al-ma'tuf) is different from the item to which it is conjoined (al-ma'tuf 'alayh). Thus one does not conjoin two equivalent things to each other, nor does one conjoin a part of a thing to the whole. And the commentator also pointed to this proof.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents a series of proofs (wujuh) supporting the view that iman is tasdiq alone. The first proof is textual: Quran and hadith consistently locate iman in the heart, and the act of the heart is nothing but tasdiq. The author anticipates an objection that these texts refer to linguistic iman (tasdiq alone) while legal iman includes more; he refutes this by stating that the word iman in the Law has been transferred (manqul) to a specific meaning, so it must be understood in that legal sense. The second proof is logical: Allah makes iman a condition for the validity of good deeds; the condition is distinct from the conditioned, so iman cannot include those deeds. The third proof is from the Quranic verse about fighting believers: Allah still calls them believers despite their sin, proving that sin does not remove iman. If works were part of iman, then leaving some works would negate iman entirely. The fifth proof (the fourth is missing) is grammatical: the conjunction ('atf) of works to iman in verses like 'those who have believed and done righteous deeds' shows they are distinct; one does not conjoin a thing to itself or a part to the whole. The commentator (al-sharh) is referenced for each proof, indicating reliance on the commentary tradition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the linguistic meaning of iman (tasdiq) is insufficient for legal iman, and that legal iman includes more than tasdiq.
Correction: The author argues that the legal meaning of iman is transferred (manqul) from the linguistic meaning, and in the discourse of the Law, it must be understood as tasdiq alone.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof that iman is solely tasdiq?
A) Quranic verses place iman in the heart

C) Believers are called believers despite fighting

Key Takeaway
Al-Laqqani uses scriptural, conditional, contextual, and grammatical arguments to demonstrate that iman is exclusively inner assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first proof states that the heart is the locus of faith. When you pray or give charity, do you ever stop to ask whether your heart truly attests to what you are doing? If Allah holds you to that inner tasdiq alone, what would happen to your deeds?
?
The second proof says iman is a condition for good deeds. This means your deeds only count if you have inner belief. Does this thought humble you? How would it change your focus in worship?
?
The fifth proof uses the word 'and' to separate belief from action. If you have been conflating your identity as a believer with your actions, how might this verse free you from despair over your sins—or, conversely, make you more careful about your inner state?

The Majority's Proofs Refuted
The author enumerates three scriptural proofs, rejects them, and refutes the Mu'tazilah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the majority's scriptural proofs for the conditionality of faith and works, and how this argument serves as an obligatory reply against the Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
The majority's own proofs collapse when consistently applied.

In this passage, the author lists three Qur'anic verses that the majority (al-jumhūr) cite to prove that faith is conditional upon works. Instead of accepting them, he declares them 'not acceptable' and explains the logical flaw: if works are part of faith, then all legislated works must be included, but the majority themselves do not hold that. This critique also strikes at the Mu'tazilites, who rely on reason alone. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs
Read the passage and note the three Qur'anic verses the majority uses: one about fasting (2:183), one about believers and righteous deeds (2:25), and one about mixing faith with injustice (6:82). Each is meant to show that faith and works are separable or conditional.
The verses are classic proof texts in this debate.
2
Understand the author's rejection
The author says these proofs are 'not acceptable'. His reasoning: if works are part of the concept of faith (as the majority claims), then all works legislated by Allah must be included. Excluding any single work would logically exclude all, since there is no distinction among works.
This is a logical inconsistency ( reductio ad absurdum ).
3
Connect to the Mu'tazilah refutation
The author adds that this reasoning also refutes the Mu'tazilites, who believe reason alone can know good and bad. If reason were sufficient, there would be no need to specify works through revelation; but the very existence of revealed commands shows that reason is not enough.
This is an 'obligatory reply' (jawāb ilzāmī) – a forced logical conclusion.
4
Reflect on the argument's force
Consider why the author calls this a 'jawāb ilzāmī'. It forces the opponent into a corner: either accept that all works are part of faith (which the majority don't) or abandon the conditionality claim. Either way, their position is undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة الجمهور في ترجيح القول بالشرطية كقوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ﴾ [البقرة: 183] وعلى أنَّ الإيمان والأعمال أمران يتفارقان، كقوله تعالى: ﴿الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ﴾ [البقرة: 25] وعلى أنَّ الإيمان والمعاصي قد يجتمعان كقوله تعالى: ﴿الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَلَمْ يَلْبِسُوا إِيمَانَهُم بِظُلْمٍ﴾ [الأنعام: 82]. غير مقبول، لأن الأعمال المأخوذة في مفهوم الإيمان عندهم هي كل الأعمال التي شرعها الله تعالى، فإذا خرج واحد منها خرج كلها، إذ لا فرق بين عمل وعمل، وهذا أقوى في الرد على المعتزلة لأنهم يرون أنَّ العقل وحده كافٍ في إثبات الأحكام بما يراه من الحُسن والقبح، إذ لو ثبت هذا كما يقولون لما كانت هناك داعية لإخراج أي جزء من أجزاء العمل سواء في ذلك ما مضى وما يأتي بعد على لسان الشرع؛ لأن ما سيأتي فيما بعد على لسان الشرع ثابت بالعقل عندهم قبل الشرع، وهذا الكلام على هذا الوجه جواب إلزامي.
The proofs of the majority (al-jumhūr) in preferring the statement of conditionality: like His saying, the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed for you' [al-Baqarah 2:183]; and that faith (īmān) and works (aʿmāl) are two things that separate, like His saying, the Exalted: 'those who believe and do righteous deeds' [al-Baqarah 2:25]; and that faith and sins may be combined, like His saying, the Exalted: 'those who believe and do not mix their faith with injustice' [al-Anʿām 6:82]. It is not acceptable, because the works taken in the concept of faith according to them are all the works that Allah has legislated; so if one of them is excluded, all of them are excluded, as there is no difference between one work and another. And this is stronger in refuting the Muʿtazilah (al-Muʿtazilah) because they see that reason alone is sufficient in establishing rulings based on what it perceives of good and bad. For if this were established as they say, there would be no motive to exclude any part of works, whether those that have passed or those that come later in the tongue of the Law; because what comes later in the tongue of the Law is, according to them, established by reason before the Law. And this speech in this manner is an obligatory reply (jawāb ilzāmī).
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents a series of scriptural proofs used by the majority (al-jumhūr) to support the view that faith (īmān) is conditional upon works—meaning faith and works are separate but required. The author cites three Qur'anic verses: one addressing believers to fast (indicating that the command to fast is directed to believers, so works are additional to faith); another juxtaposing 'those who believe and do righteous deeds' (showing they are distinct); and a third mentioning those who do not mix their faith with injustice (implying faith can coexist with sin, so faith is not itself all works). The author then states that these proofs are 'not acceptable' (ghayr maqbūl) and explains why: if works are part of the definition of faith as the majority claims, then all legislated works must be part of it, and excluding any one work would exclude all—yet the majority does not consider all works part of faith. This contradiction is used to refute the Muʿtazilites, who rely on reason alone to determine good and bad. The author argues that if the Muʿtazilite position were sound, there would be no need for prophetic law to specify any work, since reason would already know all. Thus, the presented argument is an 'obligatory reply' (jawāb ilzāmī) that forces the opponent into a logical bind.

Key Terms
أدلة الجمهور
الشرطية
الإيمان
الأعمال
المعتزلة
جواب إلزامي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is affirming the majority's proofs and using them to support conditionality.
Correction: The passage actually declares the proofs 'not acceptable' and uses them to highlight an inconsistency in the majority's own position, while also refuting the Mu'tazilah.

Checkpoint
Why does the author regard the majority's proofs as 'not acceptable' (ghayr maqbūl)?
A) Because the Qur'anic verses are not authentic.
B) Because if works are part of the definition of faith, then all legislated works must be included, but the majority excludes some.
C) Because the Mu'tazilites have a stronger argument.
D) Because the proofs contradict each other.

Key Takeaway
The author rejects the majority's scriptural proofs for conditionality by pointing out that their own definition of faith cannot selectively include only some works, and this argument also undermines the rationalist position of the Mu'tazilah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you selectively apply some Islamic commands while ignoring others, just as the majority selectively included only some works in their proofs? Which command do you find hardest to obey consistently?
?
The Mu'tazilah trusted their reason over revelation. When have you prioritized your own rational judgment over a clear text from the Qur'an or Sunnah? What was the outcome?
?
The author's 'obligatory reply' left no escape for the opponent. Is there an area of your faith where you have been resisting the truth? What would 'submission' (islām) look like in that area today?
