Rational Proof for Increase and Decrease of Faith
The author provides a rational argument proving that iman increases and decreases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that iman (faith) increases and decreases, and why this conclusion holds regardless of how iman is defined.

Core Idea
Faith must vary; otherwise, sinners would equal prophets.

This passage presents a pivotal rational argument for the increase and decrease of iman. The author acknowledges that if iman includes actions, the variation is obvious from everyday observation. But even if iman is purely inner assent (tasdiq), reason compels us to accept variation, lest we equate the sinful believer with the prophets. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice how the author structures the argument in two scenarios.
Focus on the conditional phrasing: 'if we carry iman as...'
2
Distinguish the two definitions of iman
Identify the two definitions: (1) iman as actions alone or actions plus tasdiq and iqrar; (2) iman as tasdiq alone. Understand why the proof differs for each.
3
Understand the observation-based proof
For the first definition, the author says observation and mutawatir reports suffice. Notice the example of the same person varying in obedience over time.
This is a direct empirical argument.
4
Grasp the rational reductio argument
For the second definition, the author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if iman were equal, the worst sinner would have the same iman as prophets, which is absurd. Therefore, iman must increase and decrease.
This is a rational proof independent of observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول: إنَّنا نختار القول بزيادة الإيمان ونقصانه على أي معنى من المعاني حملنا الإيمانَ ، فأمَّا إذا حملنا الإيمان: على أنه الطاعات وحدها أو على أنه: الطاعات التصديق والإقرار ; فالقول بزيادته ونقصانه لا يحتاج إلى استدلال لأنَّ المشاهدة والأخبار المتواترة يدلان دلالة قاطعة على أن بعض المكلفين أكثر من غيرهم، فيكون إيمانُ مَن كثرت طاعته زائدا على إيمان من قلت طاعته، بل الشَّخص الواحد تكثر طاعته أحيانًا وتقِلُّ طاعته أحيانًا أخرى، فهو في الحين الذي تكثر فيه طاعته أزيد إيمانًا عنه في الحين الذي تقلُّ طاعته فيه ، وأمَّا إذا فسَّرنَا الإيمان بالتصديق وحده ، فنحن في حاجة إلى الاستدلال على أنّه مع هذا يزيد وينقص ، ونقول: الدليل على أن الإيمان يزيد وينقص مُستَمدُّ مِن العقل ونصوص الكتاب الكريم ونصوص السنة النبوية: العقل: فلأنا لو لم نقل بزيادة الإيمان ونقصانه لكان إيمان آحاد النَّاس من هذه الأمة بل لكان إيمان المنهمك في الفسوق والمعصية مساوياً لإيمان الأنبياء والملائكة والصدّيقين، وهذا ممَّا لا ينبغي أن يذهب إليه أحد، ولما كان المصير إلى افتراق إيمان من ذكرنا من الأنبياء والملائكة وإيمان المنهمك في مَلَذَّاته وشهواته أمرًا لا مندوحةَ عنْهُ ؛ لزمنا القول بأنَّ الإيمان يزيد وينقص .
We say: Indeed we choose the statement that iman increases and decreases according to any meaning we carry iman upon. As for if we carry iman to be acts of obedience alone or as acts of obedience, tasdiq, and iqrar, then the statement of its increase and decrease does not need evidence because observation and mutawatir reports definitively indicate that some of the morally responsible have more [obedience] than others; thus the iman of one whose obedience is abundant is more than the iman of one whose obedience is little. Rather, the same person has abundant obedience sometimes and little obedience other times, so he is more in iman at the time when his obedience is abundant than at the time when his obedience is little. As for if we interpret iman as tasdiq alone, then we need evidence that with this [definition] it increases and decreases. We say: The evidence that iman increases and decreases is derived from reason, the texts of the noble Book, and the texts of the prophetic Sunna. Reason: If we did not say that iman increases and decreases, then the iman of individual people of this nation—rather the iman of the one immersed in sin and disobedience—would be equal to the iman of the prophets, angels, and siddiqin. This is something no one should go to. Since the necessity of distinguishing the iman of those we mentioned—the prophets and angels—and the iman of the one immersed in his pleasures and desires is unavoidable, we are compelled to say that iman increases and decreases.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the increase and decrease of iman. The author first clarifies that if iman is defined as actions (with or without tasdiq and iqrar), the increase is obvious from observation and tawatur. Even the same person varies in obedience. The real need for proof arises when iman is defined as tasdiq alone. The rational proof is an argument from reductio ad absurdum: if iman were equal for all believers, then the worst sinner would have the same iman as prophets and angels, which is absurd. Therefore, iman must increase and decrease.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if iman is only tasdiq (inner assent), it cannot change because assent is either present or absent.
Correction: The rational proof shows that even on the definition of iman as tasdiq alone, increase and decrease are necessary to avoid equating sinful believers with prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is the rational proof for increase and decrease of iman needed?
A) When iman is defined as actions alone
B) When iman is defined as tasdiq alone
C) When iman is defined as tasdiq and actions
D) In all definitions

Key Takeaway
Iman must increase and decrease, as proven by observation (if actions are included) and by reason (even if only inner assent).

?

?

The Rational and Transmitted Proofs for Iman's Increase and Decrease
Presents rational and scriptural proofs for iman's increase and decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational (ʿaqlī) and scriptural (naqlī) arguments used by the majority of Sunni scholars to prove that faith (īmān) can increase and decrease.

Core Idea
Iman must vary because if it didn't, a sinner's faith would equal a prophet's.

This lesson examines the classical Sunni proof that īmān is not static but increases and decreases. The author presents two types of evidence: a rational argument (al-ʿaql) based on logical impossibility, and transmitted evidence (al-naql) from the Qur'an and Ḥadīth. Together they establish that faith is dynamic and variable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the rational proof, then the transmitted proofs.
The rational proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the rational proof
Identify the antecedent (ملزوم) and consequent (لازم). The argument assumes non-differentiation of iman leads to equality of all believers, which is false.
The author explains the logical terms after the argument.
3
Examine the transmitted proofs
Look at the three texts: the Qur'anic verse, the hadith with Ibn 'Umar, and the hadith about Abu Bakr. Each shows increase, implying decrease is also possible.
The conclusion: whatever accepts increase accepts decrease.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how reason and revelation together support the majority view. This is a standard kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محتجين على ذلك بالعقل والنقل :
[أدلة الجمهور على قبول الإيمان الزيادة والنقص]
أمّا العقل : فلأنَّه لو لم تتفاوت حقيقة الإيمان لكانَ إيمانُ آحادِ الأمة - بل المُنْهمكين في الفسق والمعاصي - مُساويًا لإيمان الأنبياء والملائكة الصَّلاة والسَّلام ، واللازم باطل فكذا الملزوم (٢).
واللازم هنا هو: كون إيمان آحاد الأمة مساويا لإيمان الأنبياء والملائكة. والملزوم: عدم تفاوت الإيمان.
وأمَّا النَّقل : فلكثرة النُّصوص الواردة فى هذا المعنى، كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا تُلِيَتْ عَلَيْهِمْ ءَايَتُهُ زَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَنَا﴾ [الأنفال: ٢] وقوله لابن عمر سأله: الإيمان يزيد وينقص ؟ قال : نعم ، يزيد حتَّى يُدخل صاحبَه الجنّة ، وينقص حتّى يُدخل صاحبَه النَّار » (٣) وقوله : «لو وزن إيمان أبي بكر بإيمان هذه الأمة لرجح به » (٤) وكلّ ما يقبل الزيادة يقبل النقص ، فيتم الدليل .
Arguing for that by reason and transmission: [Evidence of the majority for acceptance of iman's increase and decrease] As for reason: because if the reality of iman did not differ, then the iman of ordinary members of the community - indeed those immersed in immorality and sins - would be equal to the iman of the prophets and angels, peace be upon them. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. And the consequent here is: the iman of ordinary members being equal to the iman of prophets and angels. And the antecedent is: the non-differentiation of iman. As for transmission: because of the abundance of texts that have come concerning this meaning, such as His statement, the Exalted: 'And when His verses are recited to them, they increase them in faith' [al-Anfal: 2], and his statement - regarding Ibn 'Umar who asked him: Does iman increase and decrease? He said: Yes, it increases until it enters its possessor into Paradise, and decreases until it enters its possessor into Hellfire, and his statement: 'If the iman of Abu Bakr were weighed against the iman of this ummah, he would outweigh it.' And everything that accepts increase accepts decrease, so the proof is complete.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence for the majority view that iman increases and decreases. It is a proof section containing two types of evidence: rational and transmitted. The rational proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if iman were not variable (i.e., if its reality were uniform), then the iman of any ordinary sinful person would be equal to that of the prophets and angels. Since that is impossible (the consequent is false), the antecedent (the claim that iman does not differ) is also false. The author then explains the logical terms: the consequent is the equality of iman, the antecedent is the non-differentiation. The transmitted evidence consists of three texts: a Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Anfal stating that recitation of verses increases faith; a hadith in which the Prophet confirms to Ibn 'Umar that iman increases and decreases, leading to Paradise or Hell; and a hadith saying that Abu Bakr's iman outweighs that of the whole ummah. The author concludes that since increase is proven, decrease is also entailed, so the proof is complete.

الأنبياء
الملائكة
الآية
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rational proof is circular.
Correction: Actually it is a valid reductio ad absurdum, not circular.

Checkpoint

C) The Prophets themselves said their iman increased
D) All Muslims agree that iman varies

Key Takeaway
Iman is not static; it increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience, as proven by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own iman today, can you honestly point to a moment it increased or decreased? What caused it, and what does that reveal about your relationship with Allah? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to increase it.
?

?
The rational proof shows that if iman were static, a sinner's faith would equal a prophet's. Do you live as though your iman is static—not changing with your actions? How does this truth force you to reconsider the gravity of even small sins and the value of small acts of obedience? What will you do differently starting today?

Faith Does Not Increase or Decrease: The Hanafi Proof and Response
Hanafi definitional proof against increase/decrease in faith and their exegetical response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical basis for the Hanafi position that faith does not increase or decrease, and how they interpret scriptural evidence for increase.

Core Idea
Faith as definitive assent cannot change; only its objects can increase.

In this passage, the author presents the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his school that faith is a fixed state of certainty. They argue that any change would imply it was never certain. Then they address the scriptural evidence for increase, reinterpreting it as an increase in what is believed, not in the belief itself. This lesson unpacks their argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Define faith as definitive assent
Read the definition of faith as 'assent that reaches the limit of certainty and submission.' This is the foundation of the argument.
Notice the terms 'جزم' (certainty) and 'إذعان' (submission).
2
Follow the logical implication
If faith is definitive, it cannot logically admit increase or decrease. Any change would imply it was not definitive to begin with.
This is a purely rational argument based on the definition.
3
Address the objection regarding acts
The passage notes that if faith were a name for acts of obedience, then it would vary. But faith is distinct from acts; acts do not affect the core assent.
This clarifies that faith is not synonymous with good deeds.
4
Examine the exegetical response
The Hanafis explain that scriptural references to 'increase' refer to an increase in the number of things believed (new revelations), not an increase in the strength of faith itself.
They point to the Companions' gradual belief in new rulings as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجة القائلين بعدم قبول الإيمان الزيادة والنقص (وَقِيلَ) أي : وقال جماعةٌ من العلماء أعظمُهم الإمام أبو حنيفةَ وأصحابه وكثير من المتكلمين : الإيمان لا يزيدُ ولا ينقصُ ؛ لأنَّه اسمٌ للتصديق البالغ حدَّ الجزم والإذعان ، وهذا لا يتصوّر فيه ما ذُكر ، فالمصدِّقُ إِذَا ضَمَّ إلى تصديقه طاعةً أو ارتكب معه مَعصيةً فتصدِيقُه بحاله لم يتغيَّر أصلا ، وإنَّما يتفاوتُ إذا كانَ اسماً للطَّاعات المُتفاوتة قِلَّة وكثرة . وأجابوا عمَّا تمسك به الأوَّلون : بأنَّ المُرادَ : الزَّيادةُ بحسب زيادةِ ما يُؤمن به ، والصحابة كانوا آمنوا في الجملة ، وكانت الشريعة لم تتم، وكانت الأحكام تَنزِلُ شيئًا فشيئًا ، فكانوا يؤمنون بكل ما يتجدد منها.
The argument of those who say there is no acceptance of increase and decrease in faith: (And it was said) that a group of scholars, the greatest of them Imam Abu Hanifa and his companions and many theologians, said: Faith does not increase nor decrease; because it is a name for the assent that reaches the limit of certainty and submission, and this does not admit what was mentioned. So the assenter, when he adds to his assent an act of obedience or commits with it an act of disobedience, his assent remains as it is, unchanged at all. It only varies if it is a name for acts of obedience that vary in fewness and manyness. And they answered what the first group adhered to: that the intended meaning is increase according to the increase of what one believes in. The Companions believed in summary, and the Sharia was not yet complete, and the rulings came down little by little, so they believed in each new thing that came from it.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of those who deny that faith increases or decreases, primarily Abu Hanifa and his followers. It begins by defining faith as 'assent that reaches the limit of certainty and submission.' Because faith is defined as a state of definitive conviction, it cannot logically increase or decrease—any change in conviction would imply it was not definitive. The author then addresses a potential objection: if faith is a name for acts of obedience, then it would vary as acts vary. But the response is that faith is not synonymous with acts; acts are separate. The passage then answers the proof of the first group (those who affirm increase and decrease) by explaining that the increase mentioned in scripture refers to the increase in objects of belief (i.e., new rulings to be believed in), not an increase in the quality of faith itself. The Companions believed in the basics, and as new revelations came, they believed in each new command. This shows increase in scope, not intensity.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق
جزم
إذعان
زيادة
نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that the increase in faith mentioned in the Quran refers to an increase in the strength or quality of one's belief.
Correction: The Hanafis argue that the increase refers to an increase in the number of things believed in (i.e., new revelations and rulings), not an increase in the intensity of faith itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do Abu Hanifa and his followers say faith does not increase or decrease?
A) Because faith is defined as definitive assent, which cannot change.
B) Because faith is a name for acts of obedience, which are fixed.
C) Because the Quran explicitly says so.
D) Because the Companions all agreed on this.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position rests on defining faith as definitive assent, which logically precludes increase/decrease; scriptural references to increase are interpreted as increase in objects of belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is Impossible for Allah
Introduces the third rational judgment: impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division of rational judgment: what is impossible for Allah, and how it completes the threefold classification of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah cannot have opposites to His perfect attributes, like being in a direction.

We have already learned what is necessary for Allah (wājib) and what is possible for Him (jā’iz). Now we come to the third and final division: what is impossible (mustaḥīl). This passage clarifies that the opposite of any of Allah's perfect attributes is impossible—for example, being located in a direction. Understanding this helps us affirm His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line
Read the line of poetry: 'And the opposite of the attributes is impossible for Him, His right, like being in directions.' This states the core ruling.
The line is the matn (text) of the poem, meant to be memorized.
2
Understand the commentary on structure
The commentator explains that the author previously summarized the three categories (wājib, jā’iz, mustaḥīl) and is now detailing them in order. Note that the impossible is second in detail but third in the summary.
This shows the organizational logic of the poem.
3
Identify the application of the principle
Apply the principle: since Allah has attributes like knowledge, power, and life, their opposites (ignorance, weakness, death) are impossible. Being in a direction is given as an example because it would imply limitation.
Other examples include needing help or having a physical body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسمُ الثَّالثُ مِن أقسَامِ الحُكمِ العقليّ: ما يَستَحيلُ عَليه تَعَالَى]
٤٣ - وَيستحِيلُ ضِدُّ ذي الصِّفاتِ حَقِّهِ كالكوْنِ في الجِهاتِ
ثمَّ شرعَ في ثالث أقسام (۲) الحكم العقلي المتعلقة به تعالى ، المُتقدِّمة في قوله:
=
فكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شرعًا وَجَبا عَلَيه أَنْ يَعرِفَ مَا قَد وَجَبا
إن كلا منهما يطلق بطريق الحقيقة على الصفة القديمة وعلى الألفاظ ، وهو الراجح، وعليه فيحتاج للتمييز إلى قرينة وقيل : إن كلا منهما حقيقة في الصفة القديمة مجاز في الألفاظ ، والقرآن بالعكس. الشنواني.
·
(۲) أجمل المصنف ما يجب على المكلف أن يعرفه بالنسبة الله تعالى، فيما سبق بقوله: «فكلُّ كُلِّف شَرْعا وَجَبا * عَليه أَنْ يَعرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبا * الله ، والجائز والمُمْتَنِعَ» فذَكَرَ الواجب مَن في حقه سبحانه أول ما تجب معرفته، ثمَّ عقبه بذكر الجائز في حقه سبحانه ، ثُمَّ ذكر أنه يجب على المُكلَّف معرفة ما يستحيل عليه تعالى، وعبر معرفة ما يستحيل عليه تعالى، وعبّر عنه بالممتنع، هذا في الإجمال السابق ، فلما أراد تفصيل ما تجب معرفته بدأ منها بالواجبات، ثم أردفه بذكر المستحيل، فهو ثانٍ في التفصيل ، وثالث في الإجمال المتقدم، فاعرف ذلك. محي.
The third division of rational judgment: what is impossible for Him Exalted. 43 - And the opposite of the attributes is impossible for Him, His right, like being in directions. Then he began the third of the divisions of rational judgment related to Him Exalted, which preceded in his saying: So everyone who is charged legally must know what is obligatory. Both of them are applied by way of reality to the eternal attribute and to the utterances, and this is the preponderant, and accordingly a context is needed for distinction, and it was said: Both are reality in the eternal attribute and metaphor in the utterances, and the Quran is the opposite. (2) The author summarized what is obligatory for the legally responsible person to know regarding Allah the Exalted in what preceded in his saying: 'So everyone who is charged legally must know what is obligatory: Allah, and the possible and the impossible.' So he mentioned the obligatory regarding Him, may He be glorified, first what must be known, then he followed it with mentioning the possible regarding Him, may He be glorified, then he mentioned that it is obligatory for the legally responsible person to know what is impossible for Him Exalted, and he expressed it as the impossible. This is in the previous summary. When he wanted to detail what must be known, he began with the obligatory, then followed it with mentioning the impossible, so it is second in detail and third in the previous summary, so know that.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage begins a new section: the third division of rational judgment concerning what is impossible for Allah. The commentator first quotes the line of poetry: 'And the opposite of the attributes is impossible for Him, His right, like being in directions.' He then explains that this is the third division, following the earlier statement in the poem that the legally responsible person must know Allah, and the possible and the impossible. The author of the commentary (likely al-‘Amīr al-Kabīr) notes that the poem had previously summarized these three categories. Now, when detailing them, the author first treated the necessary (wājib) attributes, then moved to the possible (jā’iz), and now the impossible (mustaḥīl). The passage also includes a brief discussion about the terms 'Quran' and 'speech of Allah' being used as reality (ḥaqīqa) for both the eternal attribute and the utterances, with the preponderant view requiring a contextual clue to distinguish. It ends with a note from the commentator about the structure of the poem: the impossible attributes are second in detail but third in the original summary.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'impossible for Allah' means things that are difficult for Him, or that the categories of necessary, possible, and impossible are arbitrary.
Correction: Impossible here is a rational judgment: certain attributes (like being in a direction) are logically impossible for Allah because they contradict His perfection and necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the third division of rational judgment discussed in this passage?

Key Takeaway
The third rational judgment is that certain attributes are impossible for Allah, such as being in a direction, and this completes the threefold classification of divine attributes.

The Three Categories and Their Order
The commentator compares the order of categories in summary vs detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment (wajib, ja'iz, mumtani') and how they are ordered differently in the summary and detailed exposition of the poem.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are the framework for knowing Allah.

This passage from the commentary helps you navigate the poem's structure. The poet listed three categories — wajib, ja'iz, and mumtani' — but their order shifts between the summary and the detailed explanation. Understanding this will prevent confusion as you study the attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without analysis. Notice the key terms: الواجب, الجائز, الممتنع. Then read the translation to grasp the commentator's point about order.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three categories in the summary
Locate where the commentator lists the three categories in the summary: 'فكلُّ كُلِّف شَرْعا وَجَبا * عَليه أَنْ يَعرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبا * الله ، والجائز والمُمْتَنِعَ'. Note the order: first wajib (Allah), then ja'iz, then mumtani'.
The summary uses 'الممتنع' for impossible.
3
Compare with the order in the detailed exposition
The commentator says: 'فلما أراد تفصيل ما تجب معرفته بدأ منها بالواجبات، ثم أردفه بذكر المستحيل'. In the detail, the author starts with wajib, then follows with mustahil. The ja'iz is not mentioned here; it appears elsewhere.
Notice the shift: in the detail, the impossible comes second, not third.
4
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Understanding the different orders helps you follow the poem's structure and avoid confusion. The commentator explicitly says: 'فهو ثانٍ في التفصيل، وثالث في الإجمال المتقدم'. This is a key methodological insight.
This shows the importance of paying attention to the commentator's explanatory notes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) أجمل المصنف ما يجب على المكلف أن يعرفه بالنسبة الله تعالى، فيما سبق بقوله: «فكلُّ كُلِّف شَرْعا وَجَبا * عَليه أَنْ يَعرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبا * الله ، والجائز والمُمْتَنِعَ» فذَكَرَ الواجب من في حقه سبحانه أول ما تجب معرفته، ثمَّ عقبه بذكر الجائز في حقه سبحانه ، ثُمَّ ذكر أنه يجب على المُكلَّف معرفة ما يستحيل عليه تعالى، وعبر معرفة ما يستحيل عليه تعالى، وعبّر عنه بالممتنع، هذا في الإجمال السابق ، فلما أراد تفصيل ما تجب معرفته بدأ منها بالواجبات، ثم أردفه بذكر المستحيل، فهو ثانٍ في التفصيل ، وثالث في الإجمال المتقدم، فاعرف ذلك. محي.
(2) The author summarized what the mukallaf (legally responsible person) must know concerning Allah the Exalted, in his preceding saying: 'So everyone who is legally obligated by the Law must know what has become obligatory: Allah, the possible, and the impossible.' So he mentioned the obligatory (wajib) regarding Him, Exalted, as the first thing that must be known, then he followed it with mentioning the possible (ja'iz) regarding Him, Exalted, then he mentioned that it is obligatory upon the mukallaf to know what is impossible (mustahil) for Him, Exalted. He expressed the knowledge of what is impossible for Him, and he expressed it as 'al-mumtani'. This is in the preceding summary. Then when he intended to detail what must be known, he began with the wajibat (obligatory attributes), then he followed that with mentioning the impossible (mustahil). So it is second in the detail, and third in the preceding summary. So know that. (Muhi)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a summary and classification. The commentator clarifies the structure of the poem's line. It explains that the poem had previously stated that the mukallaf must know three things: Allah (i.e., His necessary attributes), the possible (ja'iz), and the impossible (mumtani'). The commentator notes that the poet first listed the wajib (obligatory) attributes, then the ja'iz, and then mentioned the impossible. In the detailed exposition that follows, the poet begins with the wajib and then follows with the impossible (mustahil). So the order in the detail is: wajib first, then mustahil second (while the ja'iz is omitted or appears elsewhere). This helps the reader follow the structure of the poem.

The Permissible in Allah's Right: Definition and Explanation
Sanusi defines the category of what is permissible (jā'iz) for Allah with an example and explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what is meant by the permissible (jā'iz) with respect to Allah, and how it is defined using the example of granting wealth.

Core Idea
The permissible is what Allah may do or not do without necessity or impossibility.

In the study of rational judgments, after covering the necessary and the impossible, Sanusi now turns to the permissible. This category includes actions that Allah may choose to do or not do. In this passage, he defines the permissible with a clear example and explains the grammatical construction of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get the overall meaning without analyzing grammar.
Pay attention to the example of bestowing wealth.
2
Identify the definition of jā'iz
Locate the core definition: permissible is what is possible for us to conceive as brought into existence or annihilated. Note the example.
3
Understand the grammatical analysis
The commentary explains how the sentence is structured. Notice that 'jā'iz' is a fronted predicate and the original meaning involves a verbal noun that was omitted and then reinserted as a tamīz.
This grammatical transformation emphasizes that the action itself is what is permissible.

[
The second division of the divisions of the judgment of reason: what is permissible with regard to Him, may He be exalted.]
44 - And permissible with regard to Him is what is possible for us – bringing into existence or annihilating – like His granting wealth.
Then he began [speaking about] the second division of the preceding divisions of the judgment of reason, and said: (and permissible) as it is.
His saying: (and permissible) is a fronted predicate, and his saying: (what is possible for us) i.e., that which is possible, is a delayed subject, and his saying: (bringing into existence) is a differentiating accusative which has been transformed, while its original was a subject, and his saying: (annihilating) is conjoined to the differentiating accusative by an implicit conjunction; and the original root of this statement is: bringing into existence what is possible or annihilating it is permissible with respect to Him, may He be exalted. Then the annexed (which is 'bringing into existence') and what is conjoined to it were omitted, so the annexee was placed in its position, then the differentiating accusative (which is the omitted annexed and what is conjoined to it) was brought, so the statement became: (and what is possible is permissible with regard to Him, may He be exalted, from=)
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage defines the second division of the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-‘aqlī) regarding Allah: what is permissible (jā'iz). The verse states that the permissible is that which it is possible for us to conceive as brought into existence or annihilated by Allah, giving the example of bestowing wealth (or poverty). The commentary explains the grammatical structure: 'jā'iz' is a fronted predicate, 'mā amkanā' is a delayed subject, and 'ījādan' is a tamīz (accusative of specification) that was originally a subject in an underlying sentence. The original meaning is: 'Bringing into existence what is possible or annihilating it is permissible with respect to Allah.' The author then demonstrates how the words were rearranged by omitting the verbal noun and its conjunct, and then reinserting them as tamīz. This clarifies that the permissible actions of Allah are those that He may choose to do or not do, without necessity or impossibility. The example of granting wealth (or not) illustrates that Allah may give or withhold as He wills.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The permissible means Allah is forced to do something or not.
Correction: The permissible indicates mere possibility without necessity; Allah may choose to do or not do.

Key Takeaway
The permissible (jā'iz) for Allah is what He may do or not do, such as bestowing wealth or not, without being necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of the Possible (Ja'iz) for God
Defines the second rational judgment (ja'iz) for God with examples and grammatical analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the rational judgment of possibility (ja'iz) for God and how it applies to divine actions.

Core Idea
What is possible for God is what He may do or leave – neither is necessary.

In this passage, the author presents the second rational judgment concerning God: the possible (ja'iz). He defines it as that which can be brought into existence or annihilated, offering the example of giving wealth. The commentary then clarifies the grammatical structure to emphasize that both action and inaction are equally possible. This lesson unpacks the definition and its implications for understanding divine freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms 'ja'iz' and 'mumkin'.
The passage includes both the matn (main text) and its commentary.
2
Identify the definition of ja'iz
Find the sentence that defines what is possible for God. Note that it links to 'what we can bring into existence or annihilate'.
The definition distinguishes the possible from the necessary and impossible.
3
Examine the grammatical analysis
The commentary analyzes the wording: 'ja'iz' is a predicate, 'ma amkana' is a subject, and 'ijadan' and 'i'daman' are specifications. Understand that the original phrase was 'Bringing into existence what is possible or its annihilation is possible for Him'.
This analysis confirms that both creating and destroying are covered by 'ja'iz'.
4
Reflect on the example of rizq
Consider the example of God giving wealth and not giving wealth. Both are examples of possible actions.
The example illustrates that God is not obligated to grant or withhold wealth.

The second division of the rational judgment: what is possible for Him, exalted. 44. And what is possible for Him is what we can bring into existence or annihilate, like His giving wealth. Commentary
This passage defines the second rational judgment (ja'iz or possible) for God. It begins by identifying the second division of rational judgments concerning God: what is possible for Him. The matn line states: 'And what is possible for Him is what we can bring into existence or annihilate, like His giving wealth.' The commentary then explains why this topic is delayed: it is longer than the previous discussion on the impossible. The grammatical analysis follows: 'possible' is a delayed predicate, 'what we can' is a delayed subject, 'bringing into existence' is a converted specification originally a subject, and 'annihilating' is conjoined to it. The original phrase is 'Bringing into existence what is possible or its annihilation is possible for Him', then after grammatical transformations, the wording becomes 'And what is possible is possible for Him'. Next, the commentary on divine matters explains that the possible in reason is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid. It defines the possible for God as doing every possible thing or leaving it, expressed as 'bringing into existence' and 'annihilating'. An example is given: God's giving wealth (action) and not giving it (leaving). The footnotes clarify the pronunciation and grammatical construction of 'riziq' (provision) and the annexation structure.

Correction: The passage clarifies that possible means neither obligation nor impossibility; both action and inaction are equally valid and rational for God without necessity.

The First Rational Proof: Acts Are Created by God Alone
Presents the first rational proof: the act's possibility entails it is within God's power alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first rational proof used by Ahl al-Sunna to show that human acts are created solely by God.

Core Idea
Every possible act falls under God's power, so no act can be caused by the servant's power.

Ahl al-Sunna ground their position not only on revelation but on rational demonstration. This lesson presents the first of their rational proofs, a syllogism built on the impossibility of two effective causes sharing one effect. Follow the logic carefully: it leads to the conclusion that the servant's power produces nothing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three premises
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three premises that form the proof. The first premise is stated directly, the second is supported by earlier doctrine, and the third is actually the major premise of a second syllogism.
Pay attention to the structure: the proof is not a simple three-step argument.
2
Understand why the first premise is self-evident
The first premise states that the act of the servant is possible (mumkin) in itself. The author says this is apparent and needs no demonstration. Reflect on why the possibility of human acts is taken as obvious.
3
See how the second premise relies on God's power encompassing all possibles
The second premise asserts that every possible thing is within God's power. This is drawn from the earlier comprehensive discussion of God's power. Recognize that this is a key Sunni doctrine: God's power covers all possibles without exception.
4
Grasp the third premise and its proof
The third premise states that nothing within God's power occurs by the servant's power. Its evidence is the principle of the impossibility of two effective powers concurring on one effect. This principle prevents attributing any real causal effect to the servant's power.
This premise is actually the major premise of a second syllogism whose minor premise (the act is within God's power) is omitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل أهل السنة على ما ذهبوا إليه بالنصوص، وبوجوه من المعقول: الأول: أنَّ فعل العبد ممكن في نفسه، وكلُّ ممكن فهو مقدور الله تعالى، ولا شيء مما مقدور الله تعالى بواقع بقدرة العبد. أما المقدمة الأولى فظاهرة لا تحتاج إلى استدلال، وأما الثانية فدليلها ما مرَّ مِن شمول قدرته سبحانه للممكنات بأسرها، وأما الثالثة التحقيق كبرى قياس ثانٍ طويت صغراه لكونها نتيجة القياس الأول ـ فدليلها: امتناع اجتماع قدرتين مؤثرتين على أثر واحد ، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أنَّ فعل العبد ليس بواقع بقدرة العبد.
And Ahl al-Sunna adduced evidence for what they held from the texts and from rational aspects: The first: that the act of the servant is possible in itself, and every possible is within the power of God Almighty, and nothing that is within the power of God Almighty occurs by the power of the servant. As for the first premise, it is apparent and needs no demonstration. As for the second, its evidence is what has preceded of the comprehensiveness of His power, glorified be He, over all possibles. As for the third—which is actually the major premise of a second syllogism whose minor premise is omitted because it is the conclusion of the first syllogism—its evidence is: the impossibility of the conjunction of two effective powers on one effect. And when this is established, it is established that the act of the servant does not occur by the power of the servant.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational proof (wajh min al-ma'qul) used by Ahl al-Sunna to support their position that acts are created by God alone. The proof is a syllogism structured in three premises. The first premise states that the act of the servant is possible (mumkin) in itself. The second premise asserts that every possible thing is within God's power (maqdur Allah). The third premise concludes that nothing within God's power occurs by the servant's power. The first premise is self-evident. The second premise is supported by the earlier established doctrine of the comprehensiveness of God's power over all possible things. The third premise is actually the major premise of a second syllogism, but its minor premise (that the act of the servant is within God's power) is omitted because it follows from the first two premises. The evidence for the third premise is the principle of the impossibility of two effective powers (qudratan mu'athiratayn) concurring on a single effect. This principle, drawn from earlier discussion, prevents attributing any causal effect to the servant's power. The conclusion is clear: the act of the servant is not produced by the servant's power. Thus, the Sunni view that acts are created by God alone is rationally demonstrated.

مقدور
قدرة مؤثرة
أثر واحد
امتناع اجتماع مؤثرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third premise is an independent claim without evidence.
Correction: The third premise is actually the major premise of a second syllogism, and its evidence is the principle of impossibility of two effective powers on one effect.

Checkpoint
What is the third premise of the first rational proof, according to the passage?
A) The act of the servant is possible in itself.
B) Every possible is within God's power.
C) Nothing within God's power occurs by the servant's power.
D) The act of the servant is not within the servant's power.

Key Takeaway
The first rational proof shows that since God's power encompasses all possibles, no act can be produced by the servant's power, thereby establishing divine sole agency.

?

?
The proof shows that no act occurs by your power, but you still feel responsible. Do you live as if your actions are ultimately yours, or do you acknowledge God's sole creative power? What would change if you truly internalized this?

Second Rational Proof: The Necessity of Knowledge for Independent Creation
Begins the second proof: conditional argument on knowledge of details.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second rational proof for the Sunni position, including why knowledge of details would be required if the servant independently created his acts.

Core Idea
If you independently create an act, you must know its every detail.

The author presents a second rational argument to demonstrate that the servant cannot be the independent creator of his voluntary acts. This proof uses a conditional form: if the servant were the creator, he would need to know all details of his acts; but he does not, so he is not. The passage provides the evidence for the conditional premise, drawing on the intuitive necessity of intention and choice for specific outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the argument structure
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the proof is structured as a conditional argument (if...then).
The passage is incomplete, but the first part is clear.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The first part states: 'If the servant were the creator of his acts by choice and independence, then he would necessarily know their details.' This is the major premise.
3
Examine the evidence for the implication
The evidence explains why creation requires knowledge: every act can occur in various ways; its specific occurrence requires intention and choice, which require knowledge of possibilities and preference.
This is considered self-evident (badiha).
4
Note the incomplete minor premise
The second part begins: 'But he does not know the details of his acts,' with evidence starting from the example of the sleeping person. The passage cuts off here.
The full proof would conclude that the servant is not the creator.
5
Reflect on the logical force
Consider how this proof challenges the idea of independent human creation by showing the necessary connection between creation and detailed knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان العبد موجدا لأفعاله بالاختيار والاستقلال ؛ لوجبَ أنْ يعلم تفاصيلها ، لكنه لا يعلم تفاصيل أفعاله ؛ فلا يكون مُوجداً لها باختياره واستقلاله ، أما الملازمة التي في قولنا: «لو كان موجدا لأفعاله لكان عالما - إلخ » فدليلها : أنَّ كلَّا مِن الأَزْيَد والأَنْقَص مما أتى به ـ كان ممكنا أن يقع؛ إذ كل فعل من أفعاله يمكن وقوعه منه على وجوه متفاوتة بالزيادة والنقصان ، فوقوع الفعل على الوجه المعين، دون سائر الوجوه التي كان يمكن وقوعه عليها ؛ لأجل القصد إليه بخصوصه والاختيار المتعلق به وحده وهذا يستدعي العلم بالوجوه التي يمكن أن يقع كلُّ فعل عليها ، وبإيثار الوجه المعين دون غيره، وذلك مما تشهد به البديهة، وإذا فتفاصيل الأفعال الصادرة منه باختياره لا بد أن تكون مقصودة معلومة له. وأما الاستثنائية التي هي قولنا : لكنه لا يعلم تفاصيل أفعاله فدليلها أن النائم والساهي قد يفعل كل منهما=
The second: If the servant were the creator of his acts by choice and independence, then it would be necessary that he know their details; but he does not know the details of his acts; therefore he is not their creator by his choice and independence. As for the implication in our statement: 'If he were the creator of his acts, he would be knowledgeable, etc.' its evidence is that every increase and decrease in what he does was possible to occur; since every act of his could occur in varying ways with increase and decrease. The occurrence of the act in a specific way, rather than other ways in which it could have occurred, is due to the intention specifically directed to it and the choice connected exclusively to it. This necessitates knowledge of the ways in which each act could occur, and the preference of the specific way over others, and this is attested to by intuition. Therefore, the details of the acts issuing from him by his choice must be intended and known to him. As for the conditional statement which is our statement: 'But he does not know the details of his acts,' its evidence is that the sleeping and the inattentive one may each do...=
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage begins the second rational proof for the Sunni position. The proof is structured as a conditional argument (istithna'i). The first part establishes the conditional premise: if the servant were the creator (mujid) of his acts independently and by choice, then he would necessarily know their details. The evidence for this implication is that every act can occur in various ways (e.g., with increase or decrease), and its actual occurrence in a specific way requires intention and choice directed specifically to that way, which in turn requires knowledge of all possible ways and the preference of one. Intuition (badiha) confirms this. Therefore, the details of voluntary acts must be intended and known to the agent. The second part is the minor premise: but the servant does not know the details of his acts. The evidence for this, though cut off, is said to be that the sleeping person and the inattentive one may each do... (the text breaks off). The conclusion would be that the servant is not the creator of his acts independently. Because the passage is incomplete, the full proof and its conclusion are not present.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that the servant has any knowledge of his actions.
Correction: The proof only argues that the servant does not know all precise details of his acts, which would be necessary if he were the independent creator. It does not deny limited knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof claim that if the servant were the creator of his acts, he would need to know their details?
A) Because every act is predetermined and he must know the predetermination.
B) Because the specific way an act occurs must be intended and chosen, which requires knowledge of alternatives.
C) Because creation requires omnipotence, which includes omniscience.
D) Because the servant must be aware of all possible acts he could create.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses the necessity of detailed knowledge to refute the idea that the servant independently creates his acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you realized that you do not know the full details of even a single action you performed today, what does that reveal about your trust in your own independence? How will you live differently tomorrow knowing that every detail of your actions is under Allah's control?
?
Think of a time you were proud of an achievement, believing you orchestrated every detail. How does this proof expose the falseness of that pride? What will you say to Allah when you stand before Him and claim ownership of your deeds?
?
The proof says that if you were the independent creator, you would need to know the infinite possibilities of each act. Since you cannot, you are not the creator. Will you now submit to the truth that Allah creates your actions, or will you insist on a illusion of autonomy that leads to ingratitude?

The First Rational Proof: Premises One and Two
States the first rational proof and its first two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two premises of the first rational proof that Allah alone creates human acts.

Core Idea
Human acts are possible, and all possibles belong to God's power.

This lesson introduces the first rational proof used by the Ahl al-Sunna. The argument is structured as a syllogism. We begin with the first two premises, which are straightforward: human acts are possible in themselves, and every possible thing is within God's power. Understanding these foundational claims is essential before moving to the crucial third premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage that states the proof. Then read the translation. Notice the three-part structure: premise one, premise two, and the conclusion that nothing within God's power occurs by human power.
The conclusion is stated immediately after the two premises.
2
Understand the first premise
The first premise is 'the act of the servant is possible in itself.' The author says this is obvious and needs no proof. Reflect on why human acts are considered 'possible' (contingent) rather than necessary.
Possible means it could exist or not exist.
3
Understand the second premise
The second premise is 'every possible is within God's power.' Its proof is based on the earlier established principle that God's power encompasses all possibles. Review that principle if needed.
This principle was established in previous lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل أهل السنة على ما ذهبوا إليه بالنصوص، وبوجوه من المعقول: الأول: أنَّ فعل العبد ممكن في نفسه، وكلُّ ممكن فهو مقدور الله تعالى، ولا شيء مما مقدور الله تعالى بواقع بقدرة العبد. أما المقدمة الأولى فظاهرة لا تحتاج إلى استدلال، وأما الثانية فدليلها ما مرَّ مِن شمول قدرته سبحانه للممكنات بأسرها، وأما الثالثة التحقيق كبرى قياس ثانٍ طويت صغراه لكونها نتيجة القياس الأول ـ فدليلها: امتناع اجتماع قدرتين مؤثرتين على أثر واحد ، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أنَّ فعل العبد ليس بواقع بقدرة العبد.
And the Ahl al-Sunna adduced evidence for what they held by textual proofs and by rational arguments: The first: that the act of the servant is possible in itself, and every possible is within the power of God Most High, and nothing that is within the power of God Most High occurs by the power of the servant. As for the first premise, it is obvious and needs no proof. As for the second, its proof is what has passed of the comprehensiveness of His power, may He be exalted, over all possibles. As for the third – which is in fact the major premise of a second syllogism whose minor premise has been omitted because it is the conclusion of the first syllogism – its proof is the impossibility of two effective powers combining on one effect. And when this is established, it is established that the act of the servant does not occur by the power of the servant.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational proof used by the Ahl al-Sunna to show that human actions are not created by human power independently. The proof is structured as a syllogism with three premises: (1) the act of the servant is possible; (2) every possible thing is within God's power; (3) nothing that is within God's power occurs by the servant's power. The first premise is self-evident. The second premise is supported by the earlier principle that God's power encompasses all possibles. The third premise is actually the major premise of a second syllogism: two effective powers cannot combine on one effect (since that would imply sharing in creation, which is impossible). Therefore, the act, being within God's power, cannot be produced by the servant's power. The argument is that if the act were by the servant's power, then two effective powers would meet on one effect, which is impossible.

The Third Premise and Impossibility of Two Effective Powers
Justifies the three premises and reveals the logical structure behind the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third premise is justified and why two effective powers cannot combine on one effect.

Core Idea
Two effective powers cannot produce the same act.

The third premise of the proof states that nothing within God's power occurs by the servant's power. This is not self-evident; it is itself the conclusion of a second syllogism. The key supporting argument is the impossibility of two effective powers meeting on one effect—if the servant had effective power over the act, then both God and the servant would be creators of the same act, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the three premises
Read the Arabic passage where the author comments on each premise. Focus on the third premise: it is called the major premise of a second syllogism.
The first premise is obvious; the second is already proven.
2
Understand the third premise's proof
The third premise is proven by the principle that two effective powers cannot combine on one effect. Why is that? Because if they did, the effect would have two creators, which is contradictory to the oneness of creation.
This principle is a key doctrine in Islamic theology.
3
See the conclusion
Once the third premise is established, the original conclusion follows: the act of the servant does not occur by the servant's power.
The proof is now complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the Ahl al-Sunna adduced evidence for what they held by textual proofs and by rational arguments: The first: that the act of the servant is possible in itself, and every possible is within the power of God Most High, and nothing that is within the power of God Most High occurs by the power of the servant. As for the third – which is in fact the major premise of a second syllogism whose minor premise has been omitted because it is the conclusion of the first syllogism – its proof is the impossibility of two effective powers combining on one effect. And when this is established, it is established that the act of the servant does not occur by the power of the servant.

This passage presents the first rational proof used by the Ahl al-Sunna to show that human actions are not created by human power independently. The proof is structured as a syllogism with three premises: (1) the act of the servant is possible; (2) every possible thing is within God's power; (3) nothing that is within God's power occurs by the servant's power. The first premise is self-evident. The second premise is supported by the earlier principle that God's power encompasses all possibles. Therefore, the act, being within God's power, cannot be produced by the servant's power. The Proof from Unawareness of Details
Second rational proof from knowledge of details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that humans do not independently create their acts, based on their lack of knowledge of the details of their actions.

Core Idea
If you truly create an act, you must know every detail; you don't, so you don't create.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from everyday experience. The author argues that if we were the independent creators of our actions, we would have full knowledge of all their details, including aspects we are unaware of. By examining examples like a sleeping person turning over, we see that we lack such knowledge. Therefore, we are not the independent originators of our acts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall conditional argument
Read the passage and identify the main conditional: 'If the servant were the originator... then he would know details... but he does not know, so he is not the originator.' This is the backbone.
The argument has two premises: an implication and a denial of the consequent.
2
Examine the proof of the implication
Focus on the author's proof that if one is an originator, he must know details. The key point: actions could occur in many ways, so choosing a specific way requires intention and knowledge.
The author calls this 'the implication' (الملازمة) and proves it by the necessity of intent.
3
Examine the proof of the subjunctive premise
Look at the examples: a sleeping person turning over without awareness, and moving a finger without sensing all its parts. These show we lack knowledge of details.
This premise is 'but he does not know the details of his acts.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Combine the premises: if originator then knowledge, but no knowledge, therefore not originator. Consider what this means for human agency.
The conclusion is that humans are not independent creators; creation is Allah's alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان العبد موجدا لأفعاله بالاختيار والاستقلال ؛ لوجبَ أنْ يعلم تفاصيلها ، لكنه لا يعلم تفاصيل أفعاله ؛ فلا يكون مُوجداً لها باختياره واستقلاله ، أما الملازمة التي في قولنا: «لو كان موجدا لأفعاله لكان عالما - إلخ » فدليلها : أنَّ كلَّا مِن الأَزْيَد والأَنْقَص مما أتى به ـ كان ممكنا أن يقع؛ إذ كل فعل من أفعاله يمكن وقوعه منه على وجوه متفاوتة بالزيادة والنقصان ، فوقوع الفعل على الوجه المعين، دون سائر الوجوه التي كان يمكن وقوعه عليها ؛ لأجل القصد إليه بخصوصه والاختيار المتعلق به وحده وهذا يستدعي العلم بالوجوه التي يمكن أن يقع كلُّ فعل عليها ، وبإيثار الوجه المعين دون غيره، وذلك مما تشهد به البديهة، وإذا فتفاصيل الأفعال الصادرة منه باختياره لا بد أن تكون مقصودة معلومة له. وأما الاستثنائية التي هي قولنا : لكنه لا يعلم تفاصيل أفعاله فدليلها أن النائم والساهي قد يفعل كل منهما باختياره فعلا كانقلابه من جنب إلى جنب آخر ونحوه وهو لا يشعر بكمية ذلك الفعل ولا كيفيته ، وأيضا المُحرَّكُ مِنَّا لأصبعه محرك لكل أجزائها لا محالة ، ولا شعور له بها، فكيف يتوهم أنه يعرف حركتها ويقصدها ؟ ومتى ثبتت كل هذه المقدمات ثبت ضرورة أنَّ العبد غير موجدٍ لأفعاله بالاختيار والاستقلال.
The second: if the servant were the originator of his acts by choice and independence, then he would necessarily know their details, but he does not know the details of his acts; therefore he is not their originator by his choice and independence. As for the implication in our statement 'if he were the originator of his acts he would know... etc.', its proof is that each of the more and the less of what he does could possibly occur; for every act of his can occur from him in different ways of increase and decrease. So the occurrence of the act in a specific way, to the exclusion of other ways in which it could occur, is due to intending it specifically and choosing it alone, and this requires knowledge of the ways in which every act can occur, and preferring the specific way over others, and that is borne witness to by intuition. Therefore, the details of the acts emanating from him by choice must be intended and known to him. As for the subjunctive premise which is our statement 'but he does not know the details of his acts', its proof is that the sleeper and the absent-minded person may each perform by his choice an act, such as turning from one side to another and the like, while he is not aware of the quantity or quality of that act. Also, the one of us who moves his finger inevitably moves all its parts, but he has no awareness of them. So how can he imagine that he knows its movement and intends it? And when all these premises are established, it is necessarily established that the servant is not the originator of his acts by choice and independence.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational proof against human independent creation of actions. It is structured as a conditional argument: (1) If humans independently originate their acts, they would know all the details of those acts (because choosing a specific way among many possibilities requires knowledge of the possibilities and the chosen one). (2) But humans do not know the details of their acts (as shown by examples: a sleeping person turns over without awareness of quantity/quality; a person moving a finger does not know the movements of all its parts). Therefore, humans do not independently originate their acts. The author carefully breaks down the conditional and subjunctive premises with their respective proofs. The first premise is supported by the fact that any act can occur in various ways (more or less), and choosing a specific way requires intention and knowledge. The second premise is supported by obvious examples from everyday experience.

Key Terms
موجد
تفاصيل
اختيار
استقلال
ملازمة
استثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We know enough details of our actions to say we create them.
Correction: The argument shows we lack knowledge of complete details (like all parts of a finger movement), meaning we are not independent creators.

Checkpoint
What example is given to show that a person does not know the details of his actions?
A) A person writing a book
B) A sleeping person turning over
C) A person eating food
D) A person walking

Key Takeaway
The lack of detailed knowledge of our actions proves that we are not their independent creators; rather, Allah is the Creator of all acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today: did you know every single detail of how it happened—every muscle movement, every nerve signal? If not, how can you claim that action was solely your creation? What does your unawareness reveal about your true role in that action?
?
Recall a moment when you acted automatically, like walking while distracted. If you were not conscious of the details, yet the action still occurred, who created that action? How should this shift your reliance from your own will to Allah's power?
?
The proof shows that even the sleeper's turning involves choice but not detailed knowledge. Are there areas of your life where you assume you have full control, but in reality you lack awareness? How can acknowledging this help you surrender more deeply to Allah's sovereignty over your every act?

Two Meanings of 'Amal and the Concept of Kasb
The commentator distinguishes the two meanings of 'amal and explains kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of 'amal in Ash'ari kalam and the distinction between kasb (acquisition) and maksub (acquired act).

Core Idea
Action has two layers: the power-attachment (kasb) and the resulting movement (maksub).

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a crucial distinction in Ash'ari theology: the term 'amal (action) is used in two different senses. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on human acquisition (kasb) and divine creation. Let's explore the definitions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the full translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice that the commentator uses a very precise technical vocabulary.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'amal
The passage states that 'amal is used homonymously: the source meaning (al-ma'na al-masdari) and the meaning obtained from the source (al-ma'na al-hasil bi al-masdar). Find these definitions and note their differences.
The source meaning is an accidental matter; the obtained meaning is existential.
3
Distinguish between kasb and maksub
Look for the example of the sword strike. Identify what is the kasb (acquisition) and what is the maksub (acquired act). Also note the status of the effect (athar).
The kasb is the attachment of power; the maksub is the movement; the effect is neither.
4
Understand the application to the verse
Read how the commentator applies this distinction to Quran 37:96. Grasp the Ash'ari position: the source meaning is not created by anyone, the effect is created by Allah, and the dispute is only about the existential act.
This resolves the apparent conflict in the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن العمل يطلق بالاشتراك على المعنى المصدري ، وهو : تعلق القدرة بالوجود. وعلى المعنى الحاصل بالمصدر، وهو الحركات والسكنات، فالأول أمر اعتباري، وهو غير مخلوق الله تعالى؛ لأن القدرة لا تتعلق بالأمور الاعتبارية والثاني وجودي، وهو مخلوق الله تعالى، وهو المكلف به على التحقيق، فالمكسوب للعبد المعنى الحاصل بالمصدر الذي هو: الحركات والسكنات، وأما الكسب فهو المعنى المصدري الذي هو عبارة عن تعلق القدرة بالحركات والسكنات مثلا إذا ضرب شخص آخر بسيف فقطع يده ; فالمكسوب: القطع الذي هو حركة يده ، والكسب : تعلّق قدرته بذلك القطع ، وأما أثر القطع: فليس مكسوبا ولا كسبا ، وإنما أُوخِذ به المكلف وطولب بالقصاص والدّيّة لآنه ناشئ عن مكسوبه . ويحتمل أن تكون (ما) موصولة ، و (عَمِل) صلة ، والعائد محذوفٌ لوجود شرطه وهو: انتصابه بفعل، والتقدير وخالق للذي عمله وهو معموله الشامل للمعنى الحاصل بالمصدر ، وهو الحركات والسكنات وللأثر ، والأول محلَّ النزاع بيننا وبين المعتزلة، فهو مخلوق الله عندنا، ومخلوق للعبد عندهم ، والحاصل أن المعنى المصدري ليس معمولا ومخلوقا لأحد، لا الله سبحانه، ولا للعبد ، والأثر معمول ومخلوق الله باتفاق، والخلاف إنما هو في المعنى الحاصل بالمصدر ، ويقال له: معمول، وما قيل في المتن يقال في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُم وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ﴾ [الصافات: ٩٦] الشنواني
And know that 'amal (action) is used homonymously for the source meaning (al-ma'na al-masdari), which is the attachment of power to existence, and for the meaning obtained from the source (al-ma'na al-hasil bi al-masdar), which are movements and stillnesses. The first is an accidental matter (amr i'tibari), and it is not created by Allah the Exalted, because power does not attach to accidental matters. The second is existential (wujudi), and it is created by Allah the Exalted, and it is what the responsible person is charged with according to verification. So the acquired (al-maksub) for the servant is the meaning obtained from the source, which is movements and stillnesses. As for acquisition (al-kasb), it is the source meaning which is an expression of the attachment of power to movements and stillnesses. For example, if a person strikes another with a sword and cuts his hand, then the acquired (al-maksub) is the cutting, which is the movement of his hand, and the acquisition (al-kasb) is the attachment of his power to that cutting. As for the effect of the cutting, it is neither acquired nor acquisition, but the responsible person is taken to account and demanded retaliation and blood-money because it arises from his acquired act. And it is possible that 'ma' (ما) is a relative pronoun, and 'amila (عمل) is the relative clause, and the referent is omitted due to the presence of its condition, which is its being governed by a verb, and the estimation is 'and the Creator of that which he did', i.e., his work which encompasses the meaning obtained from the source, which is movements and stillnesses, and the effect. The first is the point of dispute between us and the Mu'tazila: it is created by Allah according to us, and created by the servant according to them. The conclusion is that the source meaning is not a work and is not created by anyone, neither Allah the Exalted nor the servant, while the effect is a work and created by Allah by consensus. The disagreement is only about the meaning obtained from the source, and it is called 'work' (ma'mul). And what is said in the text is said regarding His statement: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (Quran 37:96). Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the terms 'amal (action), kasb (acquisition), and maksub (acquired thing). The commentator first clarifies that 'amal has two meanings used homonymously: the source meaning (al-ma'na al-masdari), which is the attachment of power (qudra) to existence—an accidental, non-creatable matter because power does not attach to accidents; and the meaning obtained from the source (al-ma'na al-hasil bi al-masdar), which consists of the movements and stillnesses (al-harakat wa al-sakanat)—an existential reality created by Allah. The second is what the responsible person (mukallaf) is charged with. Then the commentator distinguishes between maksub and kasb: the maksub is the produced action (e.g., the cutting of the hand), while the kasb is the attachment of the servant's power to that action. The effect (athar) of the action (e.g., the injury) is neither maksub nor kasb; however, the servant is held accountable for it because it results from his maksub. The commentator then discusses a grammatical possibility regarding the word 'ma' in the verse. He reiterates the Ash'ari position: the source meaning is not created by anyone, the effect is created by Allah by consensus, and the dispute with the Mu'tazila is only about the meaning obtained from the source (the action itself). This explanation is then applied to the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do'.

Key Terms

المعنى المصدري
المعنى الحاصل بالمصدر
الكسب
المكسوب
الأثر
القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the servant creates his own actions entirely, or that the action as a source meaning is created.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the existential act (the obtained meaning) is created by Allah; the source meaning (power attachment) is not created by anyone.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is created by Allah?
A) The source meaning (al-ma'na al-masdari)
B) The meaning obtained from the source (al-haasil bi al-masdar)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that human actions have a non-created aspect (kasb as power attachment) and a created aspect (maksub as the actual movement), reconciling divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the example of the sword strike, the striker is held accountable for the injury even though it is not his acquired act. Think of a time when you caused unintended harm. How does this passage challenge you to take responsibility for the consequences of your actions, even when you feel they were accidental? Will you actively seek to make amends rather than excuse yourself?
?
The passage distinguishes between the power-attachment (kasb) and the movement (maksub). When you perform a good deed, do you attribute the actual deed to Allah's creation or to your own effort? How does this distinction affect your humility and reliance on Allah? What one thing will you do today to align your understanding with the Ash'ari view that your action is both from Allah and from you?
?
Consider your daily sins: do you ever blame them on 'Allah creating my action' to avoid feeling guilty? The passage states that the existential act is created by Allah, but your kasb—the attachment of your will to the sin—is entirely yours. The Doctrine of Kasb: Definition and Scriptural Evidence
Definition of kasb with scriptural evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb defines human action as a proportion of preponderance, not creation, and its scriptural basis.

Core Idea
Human action is acquired, not created; the servant merely inclines.

The Ash'ari position on human free will centers on the concept of kasb. This lesson defines kasb and grounds it in explicit Qur'anic verses, showing that while Allah is the sole creator, humans have a role in preponderating towards an action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the author's definition and evidence.
Pay attention to the key terms: كسب and ترجيح.
2
Identify the definition of kasb
The author states what kasb is not (creation) and what it is (proportion of preponderance). Note the phrase 'نسبة الترجيح'.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
Two Qur'anic verses are cited. Understand how they support the doctrine that Allah is the sole creator.
Verse 1 (Al-Furqan 2) emphasizes creation and determination; verse 2 (As-Saffat 96) explicitly includes human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة كسب الأفعال غير أنْ يكونَ مُوجدا وخالقا لها ، وإِنَّما له فيها نسبة الترجيح، كالميل للفعل أو التّرك، والأصل في هذا قوله تعالى: ﴿وَخَلَقَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ فَقَدَّرَهُ تَقْدِيرًا﴾ [الفرقان: 2] . ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: 96] . ولو كانَ العبد خالِقا لأفعاله لكانَ عالما بتفاصيلها ، واللَّازِم باطل ؛ فالملزوم كذلكَ . فَلْتَعْرِفَا هذا الحُكْمَ الخَفِيَّ الإدراك ، مع ظهوره عند مُثبتِ الوحدانيّة المَحضَة له تعالى
The issue of the acquisition of actions: it is not that he is an originator and creator of them, but rather he has in them a proportion of preponderance, like inclination towards action or abandonment. The basis for this is the saying of the Most High: 'And He created everything and determined it with [precise] determination' [Al-Furqan: 2], 'And Allah created you and what you do' [As-Saffat: 96]. And if the servant were a creator of his actions, he would be knowing of their details, but the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. So know, both of you, this judgment that is hidden in perception, while it is apparent to the establisher of pure unity for Him, Exalted.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage provides both transmitted and rational proof for the doctrine of kasb (acquisition). It begins by defining the issue: human action is not created by the servant, but the servant has an aspect of preponderance (tarjih) through inclination. Then it cites two Qur'anic verses as transmitted evidence. Then it presents a rational proof in the form of a conditional syllogism: if the servant were the creator of his actions, he would have detailed knowledge of them (a necessary implication), but the consequent is false (by experience, humans do not know all intricate details of their actions), hence the antecedent (the servant being creator) is also false. The conclusion is that the servant is not the creator. The author then addresses the reader (dual form 'fa'tarifa') to recognise this subtle judgment, which is clear to those who affirm pure divine unity.

Key Terms
كسب
خالق
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb means humans create their actions alongside Allah.
Correction: The passage clearly states that the human is not an originator or creator; kasb is only preponderance.

Checkpoint

A) The human's creation of the action
B) The human's inclination towards action or abandonment
C) Allah's direct creation of every action
D) The physical details of the action

Key Takeaway
Kasb means the human has a share in action only through inclination, not creation, as supported by Qur'anic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your action is not created by you but only acquired through inclination, then what do you truly control? Reflect on a recent good deed: Did you attribute its existence to Allah or to yourself? Write one sincere intention to change that attribution.

The passage states that you have only a 'proportion of preponderance' in your actions. How does that humble you? Think of a sin you committed: was it truly your creation or your inclination? How can you ask forgiveness differently knowing that Allah creates even your deeds?
?
If Allah creates everything, including your actions, what does this mean for your sense of personal achievement? Are you willing to give up the credit you take for your good deeds? What specific act of worship will you do today to acknowledge that Allah is the sole creator of your actions?
